pre-eminence in their treatment. His practice
embraces diseases which probably cause more un-
happiness than any other class of troubles that
afflict mankind.
YOUNG MEN
who suffer from the results of early follies, trans-

Punishment—Caial and Corporal.
Pie P3ulleizoi.
SATURDAY, DECEMBER 0, 1890.
Editor Bulletin, —What is your argument in favour of
the abolition of the death penalty ? From,what I can see
you say that it does not act as a deterrent to crime. Well,
if the death penalty does not, what does? Vour argu-
ment would do away with punishment and gaols alto-
gether if carried to its logical conclusion. In fact, it
would do away with government itself. But there is no
need to go further. Capital punishment (loco, prevent
crime. How many thousands of men are there of whom
it can with perfect justice be said “ that the fear oft be
law keeps them honest? - ’ And if we admit tttat'snght
punishment prevents slight (comparatively) crimes, how
can we refuse to admit that heavy punishments will pre-
vent heavy crimes? I admit that in spine cases the mur-
derer or the debauchee itWs 'hot Hunk of the penalty
when committing the crime ; yet there must bgjMirfftrefls
who would for the’
stiingent punishmenUrtfaiting them. Fancy the state of
a country-t-bat would-five '-‘ three Tears."- for-a-tnurder.
And if you abolish the death penalty what do you propose
to substitute in its place ? Nothing that I know of that
would give a fraction of the satisfaction that the present
system has given. lam a young Australian and with you
look forward to a.great Southern independent Australian
Bepublic, but I do not wish to see crime made less easy,
or to have the lives of our citizens or the honour of our
women less protected. Up to the present we have shown
older countries that we know how to legislate. Our repre-
sentative system and our educational system are un-
rivalled in the world, and nowhere is the general intelli-
gence so high and the general prosperity so great as in
these Australian colonies. But once start weakening our
protection against crime, and our glory, instead of in-
creasing, will begin to wane. —Young Australian.
We give the foregoing protest in its entirety,
partly because it comes from one who calls
himself a “Young Australian,” and partly
by reason of its giving a fair synopsis of the
views held by many regarding capital
punishment. For the sake of convenience
it would be as well to answer in detail the
objections advanced—to answer them in
plain, every-day language, so that an
ordinary man or woman may understand the
position we have taken up from the
beginning, and which, so far, we have seen
no reason to abandon. If there were two
countries exactly equal in every respect,
with the single exception that, in one,
murder was punished with death,and, in the
other not punished at all, the assumption—
indeed, the certainty—is that (here would
be more murders in the latter than
in the former. Most punishments act
as deterrents to crime. But given four
countries, say, in the first of which boiling,
in the second burning, in the third hanging
(or other method of life-taking), and in the
fourth life-long imprisonment is the penalty
for deliberate assassination, there is abso-
lutely no reason to suppose that wilful
murder would be more common in the last
country than in the otheis. There was a
time —not quite Avithin the memory of living
men, but not very far beyond it—when the
first two methods of execution, as well as
breaking on the Avheel and other indescrib-
able modes, were in vogue. The science of
statistics is a child of this century ; but, so
far as researches have gone into the meagre
criminal records of preceding centuries,
there is good reason to suppose that the
murder-rate, according to population, has
rather diminished than increased by the
humaner modes of recent years. At all
events, this fact should never be for-
gotten —it is not so much the severity
of punishments as the certainty of discovery
that keeps men from crime. Imagine
two nations, in one of which crucifixion was
the murder penalty, while imprisonment for
life was the laav in the other. If but live
per cent, of the murderers were apprehended
in the former, Avhile sixty or seventy per
cent, were pretty sure to be arrested in the
latter, the murder rate would be high in
the crucifying land and low in the imprison-
ing one. This, then, is the cardinal fact to
be borne in mind when discussing a subject
of the present kind —it is the certainty of
discovery by means of a highly-efficient
police, rather than the barbarity of the pun-
ishment that tends to reduce crime of any
kind. Nor must it be forgotten that murder
is the only crime whose punishment is of the
same character as the offence. Therefore,
capital punishment is the most illogical of
penalties.
# # #
“If the death-penalty does not [deter
from crime], what does ?” says our corres-
pondent. Is it news to him to learn that far
into the present century the British criminal
code recognised over two hundred offences
for which hanging could be inflicted ? Did
this stop crime ? So far from that being the
case, it was a common and just taunt by
Home and Continental jurists seventy years
ago that, Avhile England loudly proclaimed
herself the freest of European countries, she
had more proportionate crime than other
lands, and hanged more people than all other
European countries put together. Had our
correspondent been alive at the time, and in
power, he would have consistently joined
with the Bench of Bishops in opposing the
repeal of the laws that made forgery a
capital offence as well as sheepstealing, shop-
lifting, poaching, and some hundreds of
others. “ If the death penalty does not
deter, Avhat does?” would have been his
logical comment before recording his vote.
The worst of it was that it did not deter.
Shops Avere lifted, names forged, hares
trapped, and housebreaking conducted on
far more scientific plans than since the larvs
Avere ameliorated and the police rendered
more efficient. Here, then, in the case of
minor offences, Ave have the indisputable
facts (a), that bloody laws did not diminish
certain offences; (?>) that humane laAvs,
coupled Avith a completer police system, did.
Is there any reason to suppose that,
in the case of murder, the same effect
Avould not follow on the same cause ? None
that avc can see. Besides, if hanging, which
in most cases is almost painless, is so
effective, Avhy should not breaking on the
Avheel or crucifixion, both of which are said
to be accompanied by much pain, bo more
effective-still ? To be consistent, theti, our
“Young Australian” should advocate the
return to these methods of death, as the pain
they occasioned would be far more deterrent
than the comparatively painless plan of
breaking the neck by hanging. Indeed,
when execution by electricity became the
law in Ncav York, some English conservative
journals, deplored the introduction of a
measure that Avould have but little effect in
suppressing crime as it promised to -guic-mr .
pain at,all,.
• #
“ Your argument Avould do away Avith
punishment and gaols altogether,”says our
correspondent:' This fiFshfious.”' It r roTfows“
from it that because Bomilly, Mackintosh
and Brougham said it Avas wrong to
hang for forgery, therefore forgers
should go unpunished. But it is pos-
sible to ■ punish a man without strang-
ling him. Again, suppose you strangle
the wrong man ? It has been done before
in numberless cases—U will be done again,
so long as witnesses and juries are human,
while judges and sometimes executives are
inhuman. To this, no doubt, “ Young
Australian ” would reply that a man more or
less matters very little, and that, as such
mistakes seldom occur, a person has to take
his chance. But, it appears to us, that, if
he happened to be the innocent man, his views
on the subject would undergo a material
change by the time he was pinioned and re-
quested to take his proper place in the proces-
sion. It would be little consolation either to
an innocent man or to his friends to be told
that a blameless person was seldom hanged,
and that, in his case, the mistake was un-
likely to occur again. It is remarkable
with what equanimity men hear of the
misfortunes of others. The same align-
ment applies to another essentially Aus-
tralian punishment—torture. If it is small
consolation to murmur over a man’s
dishonoured grave that he had been legally
murdered, it is scarcely more to whisper in
the ear of a wretch, Jimp and bloody from
(lie triangles, that another person should
have received the stripes. This brings us
to another phase of the subject—to a punish-
ment which, in some respects, is so closely
related to that termed “ capital,” that it
would be impossible to omit all reference to
it in an article such as tliis.
* # #
The punishment alluded to is that known
as corporal or, colloquially, flogging. What
appears to have escaped “ Young Austra-
lian,” is that any penalty which admits of
no reparation is wrong—intrinsically and de-
monstrably wrong. Under the Norman, Plan-
tagenet, Tudor and Stuart scoundrels, what
amends was there possible for the peasants
who were deprived ot their eyes or had their
bones crushed in the “ boot?” No one could
give back to them the organs that enabled
them to see God's fair world, or the sturdy
limbs that enabled them to enjoy it. It is
just the same with capital and corporal pun-
ishment in the Australia of to-day—in the
land of which our correspondent appears to
be so proud. Hang a man, and any future
sympathy with him or his is but mockery—
skin him, and it is much the same. It is but
two or three years ago that, for performing
a function which his judges and their forbears
had performed since Adam was ejected from
Eden, a wretched sailor was flayed in Syd-
ney ; while, for calling Moses names, a half-
daft chimney-sweep was skinned in Yass. It
is but yesterday that a Sydney magistrate
threatened to torture for common assault.
Does “Young Australian” know this, or,
knowing, justify ? Before returning to our
proper subject, that of “capital” punish-
ment, we shall be a little plain with him,
partly because it is our way, but chiefly on
account of his boast that “ up to the present
we have shown older countries that we know
how to legislate.”
* * *
We have, there is no denying it. But it
is principally by showing them not what to
follow but what to avoid. Hearken, “Young
Australian,” and all of your way of thinking.
America, Germany, France, England, and
Italy—we take them in the order of popu-
lation —contain some 220,000,000 of the
most civilised of mankind. In America the
lash is unknown except in one small State
(Delaware), where the Governor says it is an
unaccountable remnant of barbarism ; and
even in Delaware it is awarded but for one
offence—wife-beating ; in England (outside
of gaol-walls, where it is still retained for
disciplinary purposes—it is unknown except
for one or two peculiarly brutal (but not
sexual) crimes ; in the remaining Teutonic
nation, as well as in the two Latin ones, it
is unknown altogether. But in Australia—
particularly in New South Wales—it not alone
flourishes and is applied vigorously, but
shows every likelihood of having its range
extended to fresh offences in addition to the
few dozen to which it is at present applied—
betrays every promise, in fact, of reaching a
green old age. This means that while the
rascaldom of the best part of the civilised
world can be governed without torture, the
blackguardism of New South Wales cannot.
Hence, either the province’s ruffianism is
unique or the province’s methods are. It is
probable that “ Young Australian ” has never
been beyond Australia’s confines—possibly
never beyond those of his province. But it
is not nice when travelling abroad and
venturing on the vexed subject of torture
to be reminded with a smile and a shrug that
such methods might do for the convict spawn
of Australia but not for cultivated lands. If
we arc “ convict spawn ” there is no need to
proclaim it to the world by our saVage and
bloody legislation.
* # #
It is necessary to say a few words on this
branch of the subject. Up to 181(5, women
were publicly flogged in England ; so they
were in New South Wales. Byron speaks
of the trulls he saw shrieking at the cart’s
fail while he was preparing to embark on the
journey that resulted in “ Childo Harold. ”
■ Why has this tine old custom been discon-
tinjp.'d.b.ulli here and “4it,homo ” if effica-
cious ? Urn. public conscience "re-
volted againsUtlTC-1 orfuring’ of women in
public, and, ultimately, against the hang-
mg of* either men or women coram
popnlo. And before long—not, perhaps,
in Australia but in older lands—public
sentiment will revolt against strangling or
skinning culprits whether outside the gaol or
inside. A priori , the deterrent effects of
capital and corporal punishment should be
telling when seen by all. But experience has
proved the precise contrary to have been the
case. More pockets were picked, more men
garotted, and more women trampled in
the gutters during a public exhibition of
hanging and Hogging for pocket-picking
garotting, or assault than during any other
public display of whatever kind. These are
no random statements made to bolster up a
cause or a thesis. They are hard, indubit-
able facts vouched for by testimony which it
would be idle to question without question-
ing the testimony that established the death
of CLesar, or the Battle of Waterloo. What
do they teach ? This above all—that it i s
not the savagery of punishments, but the
certainty of detection, that keeps crime at
its minimum.
* * *
Our correspondent observes with ingenu,
ousness, “Fancy the state of a country that
would give three years for a murder.
And if you abolish the death penalty what
do you propose to substitute in its place?” It
is, really, a little irritating at this time of
day to have to answer questions like the
foregoing. Three years or less would have
been ample for some “murders ” for which
men have been sent to death in New South
Wales. Why three years, when natural
life could be awarded ? The man who would
hesitate to commit murder while death was
the penalty, but who would kill without
hesitation when life servitude was the law
has yet to be discovered. Of all the clap-
trap that has appeared on this subject there
is none so pitifully inane as this “What
would you substitute for hanging ?” What
course do other nations follow where the
death penalty is either entirely or practically
unknown ? Can no walls be made high
enough, cells strong enough, to confine a
person so that he may be found when
wanted ? At the present moment there are
murderers confined for life in New South
Wales gaols. Our “Young Australian”
would probably answer that, in the first
place, the State has been put to an unwarrant-
able expense in keeping and guarding
them for years ; and, in the second, that
they, are always liable to commit a fresh
murder, either of a warder or of a fellow-
prisoner, or to escape and kill one or more
persons outside. It would be better to have
hung them out of hand long ago, and so
have nipped their homicidal propensities, if
not in the bud, yet as near the bud as
possible. This argument, together with the
one which asserts that the death-penalty
deters more than any other, is the strongest
that can be brought forward in favour of
capital punishment. Both deserve examina-
tion.
* * *
To our mind, the latter— i.e., that the
death-penalty is the greatest preventive of
wilful murder —is by far the stronger of the
two, by far the strongest, indeed, of any that
can be adduced for capital punishment. If it
could be shown that, in a given community
with death as the reward for murder, the
assassinations averaged a hundred a year,
while in the same community, or in one
similar in every respect (if such a one could
be found), where life-imprisonment was the
penalty the murders averaged a hundred and
one annually, we would at once vote for the
retention of death as the - extreme punish-
ment. But on one condition only. It must
also be shown that while the death penalty
kept down the murders to the minimum, it
did not destroy the nation by hardening the
public conscience or by driving crime into
fresh channels. To explain a little more:
If, by hanging all who took life, the public
conscience or sentiment—call it what you
will was outraged and the populace
brutalised, as was the case when executions
and floggings were public and common, the
restriction of unlawful life-taking to its
minimum might be bought at too dear a price.
And, in saying that the undoubted tendency
of capital and corporal punishment in public
or in private is to indurate the national
conscience we challenge contradiction. But
it never has been shown that the prevalence
of either of these forms of punishment has
been successful in bringing to the lowest
point the offences for which it was pre-
scribed.
« # #
A point omitted by Young Australian—a
very important one to our thinking—is this :
He says nothing of the cases, so common in
England (50 years ago, and so instructively
present in New South Wales to-day, where
juries refuse to convict, although the evi-
dence, if not conclusive, is such that in the
ordinary affairs of life an average man
would not hesitate to be guided by it.
Why do the juries refuse ? Because
they are stupid, some say—because they
lean towards crime, say others. Both
sayings are grotesque in their irration-
ality. They refuse as their English brethren
anterior to 1830 did, because they know that
conviction may mean destruction to the
culprit—because, after conviction, recom-
mendations to mercy are unavailing—because,
whatever chance a culprit had of getting off
with an unstretched neck ora sound skin
when no appeal for mercy was made for him
to the Court, the very fact of appealing
would be certain to end in a broken spinal
column or an abraded cuticle.'-.. In vain the
J udge urges them to consider their oaths, and
chides them for presuming to inquire what
the sentence may be. Facts for them, law
for him. But New South Wales juries of
late have acquired an odd way of looking at
things. The province is full of free men
whose people were free years before the
progenitors of existing magistrates. So the


foreman of the jury looks out of corners
of his eyes and says “not guilty.” Then
the Judge, debarred of his hanging or his
flogging, forgets himself and flounders. He
forgets that he is a servant, and he flounders
still more. In a word, so far has the humane
public sentiment of New South Wales
advanced since the imposition of her criminal
code that the more the lash and the rope are
legalised the lower becomes the proportion of
convictions to committals. To be plain, a
certain percentage of offenders is let free to
prey upon society because colonial lawmakers
and magistrates still linger lovingly round
the triangles and the gallows, as if afraid to
take their places in the world’s procession
and join in the onward march of humanity.
* * *
This inability or disinclination to move
with the times is, indeed, the curse of de-
pendence. It took America a century of
freedom to cleanse her Statute-book from
the.foulness resulting from the undisturbed
reign of prince, prelate and peer. At one
time, convicts were sent in thousands to the
“Plantations,” so that there is no reason to
suppose that the criminal taint is stronger
to-day in Australia than in the United States.
Unfortunately, however, for us, if the bodily
taint is weak the mental is strong. Con-
victism has disappeared, but its spirit still
remains. This spirit can be seen in the pages
of our law books and heard In the speeches
of our legislators and the addresses of our
magistrates. It is doubtful if anything short
of "political independence will lay it for good.
The punishments which “ Young Australian ’
advocates so as to protect; the lives of citizens
and the honour of women have much to
answer for, not alone in allowing murderers
and ravishers to go free, but in enabling
many “astute women ” to make a first-class
living through the natural reluctance of men
to strip in presence of the scourger. As for
“ dimming Australian glory ” by abolishing
infernal punishments, it is but necessary to
say that the glory lost because no more men
were allowed to be choked or peeled would
leave the country none the worse.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
A Dead King.
Last week the cable announced that William
of Holland had gone to sleep with his fathers—
a circumstance which is lamentably rough on
William’s fathers —and the hair on which the
destinies of the Netherlands have hung for so
many years having broken, new complications
loom in the future. The Prince of Orange, son
and expectant heir of the deceased monarch,
died years ago of old age, drink, and disgust,
and now only a girl of tender years stands
between Holland and its manifest destiny-
absorption in the all-devouring German Empire.
The Dutch nation itself has no particular voice
in the matter, for the principle of monarchy is
that the people belong to the sovereign, and
when the sovereign dies they belong to the next
heir. If there is no direct heir, they belong to
whatever relative comes uppermost in the
scramble. Germany for the present receives a
portion of Luxembourg, and in consideration of
this concession it agrees to leave the rest of
Holland alone for the present, and when the
young queen conies to years of discretion
provided old William has not used up all the
available discretion of the family—there will
l>e a rush for her hand, and the rest of the
Dutch people will be the property of whoever
gets it. Possibly Albert Victor, of the con-
spicuous cuffs, may try his fortune and
absorb Holland in the British Empire,
and then there will be international
“complications,” and Germany will demand
another segment of the little kingdom as
“ compensation,” and Austria will skirmish
after a portion of Macedonia to pay her for not
interfering, and Russia will insist on some more
territory in Armenia by way of “ satisfaction.
But more probably some German prince with
six army corps behind him will bounce the pool
little Dutch queen into matrimony, and devour
the fair provinces of the Netherlands, and
should this not come to pass Holland will go at
last the way Hanover went, and the way all
small kingdoms must go, sooner or later. And
whichever way the kingdom may go the few
million inhabitants will be thrown in as an un-
jonsidered trifle of no special account. The
monarchical system takes no notice of mere in-
habitants. If it were possible for the copious
royal family of England to become extinct, then
the next heir to the Crown would bo found in
the imperial house of Germany, and according
to all existing law Australia would be an in-
tegral portion of Germany from that time forth,
quite irrespective of any views the Austra-
lian people might hold on the subject.
Even the right of formal protest is
not allowed to Australia in her piesent
situation j if the Parliament at London
were prepared to accept the situation then
this continent would have no. voice whatever in
the matter. Monarchy, no matter under what
limitations, is incurably vicious, because
it invariably assumes that the sovereign
has a status of his own apart from that
which is conferred on him by the people.
Its essential axiom is that any bloated
alien despot who chances, because he is the son
of his mother and presumably of his father, to
be next heir to a dead monarch, has an incon-
testable claim to take over a country and a
nation as the personal assets of the deceased,
and half the world’s history is made up of the
struv'des of successive races to resist the per-
sonal application of this great mis-principle. As
for abolishing the mis-principle itself, nine-tenths
of the world’s population has not even begun to
dream of such a possibility., Man has belonged
to somebody else so long that the idea of belong-
inf to himself is regarded as atheism and
anarchy. Even so-called “ Wars of Independ-
ence ” are generally only the expression of a
public desire that the nation shall become the
property of a king born at home instead of a
king born abroad, and when a nation has
resolved to be the property of somebody it is
hardly worth while to light about mere details.
«.
The Political Three-Card Trick.
Two weeks ago The Bulletin prophesied—in a
leading article with the above heading —the
way the Parses Government would go in deal,
ing with Mr. Traill’s Bill for the abolition
of plural voting, and last week the Parses
Government fulfilled that prophecy to the
letter. There is no particular merit—so far as
The Bulletin is concerned —in this circum-
stance ; the Parses Cabinet had either to take
a straight course or a crooked one, and no
Parses Cabinet ever took a straight course
within the memory of man. When Mr.
Traill’s measure came up in Committee the
followers and hangers-on of the great and un-
grammatical “Hi Ham” rose with an expres-
sion of pained surprise and talked it out, and
prominent among the reproachful voices came
the dissonant howl of Carruthers, who rallied
up the Government parasites on the ground that
an unprincipled member was endeavouring to
jump the Ministerial claim, and rob the Foreign-
trade party of the credit rightly due to it tor
the authorship of a great reform. Every mem-
ber present was, of course, well aware that Mr.
Traill’s measure was introduced long before
Parses’ Bill was heard of, that the One Man One
Vote principle had been derisively thrown out by
leaders of the Hew South Wales Freetrade Con-
ference, and that the Government had openly
recpiested Traill to withdraw his proposals
because it wanted to embezzle his project,
and pass it off as a thing that had been
festering in Parses’ brain for ages. If
Parses had accused Shakespeare of em-
bezzling the inspiration of “ Hamlet ” from the
solemn drivel of his “Murmurs of the Stream,”
it would have been a shade less absurd than the
charge levelled against the Democratic party of
stealing its own programme from its opponents,
and even the robust assurance of the father of
all political borrowers must have been put to a
severe strain under these ignominious condi-
tions. But the Foreign-trade majority, being
informed, contrary to the evidence of their own
senses, that the One Man One Vote Bill was a
Foreign-trade inspiration, and must, therefore,
be preserved from outside interference, prac-
tically snuffed out Mr. Traill’s proposals.
Then, as the next step, the old Foreign-trade
boss will push his Bill through its preliminary
stages, and reluctantly drop it for want of time
to bring the matter to completion, and when
the general election arrives—as it only will
when Parliament has run its full term —it will
find an aged political shuffler still labouring
manfully in a frantic effort to pass his great
measure, and at the same time to make sure
that it does not pass. Next he will appeal for
the vote of the Democracy because he tried to
abolish the plural vote of property, and he will
get, the vote of Property because he prevented
that same plural vote being abolished, and
should these tactics secure him another
lease of power, he will be guided afterwards
by circumstances. If he finds it possible to
spend the term of another Parliament in tre-
mendous, but unavailing, struggles to pass the
Electoral Bill, he will keep on struggling with
the unobtrusive vigour of Atlas holding up the
earth. And if, by any chance, he finds himself
getting too near success, the issue will be sud-
denly obscured by a gigantic project for annex-
ing Asia and extinguishing the term New South
Wales in order that the colony may be named
the Pacific Ocean, and for building a State
House 14,000 feet high with an inverted pyra-
mid in the back-yard, and establishing an
Imperial republic of despotic tendencies limited
by universal suffrage, the whole proceeding to
finish up with an exhibition of fireworks in the
evening. Something of this description always
happens when Parkes finds himself on the
verge of keeping any one of his many promises,
and then the old man fades away in a cloud of
dustand when the dust clears away the
country has generally forgotten what the pro-
mise was, and Sir Henry starts afresh by intro-
ducing a Bill for giving everything to everybody,
and abolishing things in general, and putting a
stop to circumstances at large.
—-—-
Mr. Stipendiary Abbott, of Sydney, had before
him last week a man charged with indecency.
The S.M. regretted that he had not another
Magistrate sitting with him, so that he could
order prisoner 48 hours’ and a flogging, but he
would send him to the Quarter Sessions, where
he would be liable to fourteen years’ penal servi-
tude— and then Mr. Abbott immediately allowed
this formidable offender bail, not in thousands,
but in two fifties ! Mr. Abbott is going away
for two months’ holiday. He has of late been
in a condition of health in which he certainly
could not be expected to discharge his magis-
terial duties with that calmness and freedom
from irritability which should ever characterise
the professional beak. The Bulletin notices
that he has of late on one or two occasions
talked pretty loudly about flogging people.
Have not the people of New South Wales suffi-
ciently recovered from lag-time influences to
see that if men are to be flogged, it should cer-
tainly only happen in accordance with the
deliberate findings of a judge and jury, instead
of in obedience to a summary decision in which a
magistrate afflicted by the peevishness of ill-
health possibly takes a chief part ?
It is proposed by a number of influential mem-
bers of the A.M.P. Society that the sum of
£2500 be voted to the ow of the late Mr.
Morrice A. Black, late chief actuary to that
great insurance office. This proposition has
been agreed to by the majority of the members
present at a special meeting in Sydney, but a
ballot, which will cost a lot of money, is to be
taken on the subject throughout the Australias.
As the late Mr. Black during his 22 years _of
service received an average salary of £100.) a
year, besides having a year and nine months’
leave of absence on full pay, and leaving behind
him a total nett estate of £12,388 in policies
and property, it is hard to see the special
grounds on which such a proposal can be justly
based. One thing is certain : When a ballot is
taken in such circumstances, those who uphold
the proposal to vote away the Society’s money
should concisely state their case in a circular
enclosed within the ballot-paper sent from the
head-office; while the objectors also should
have the privilege of making their statement
under the same cover.
Premier Munro is going on a “ teetotally ”
opposite tack to the extravagant Gillies. He
informed the young men who want to obtain
evening lectures, in connection with Melbourne
University, that it was useless to deputationise
him, as he would not appropriate the immense i
sum (£1000) required by the University Council
to meet the extra expenses of the Arts course,'
The poor devils who have to work for their
living, and who are consequently far better men
than those who don’t, have to pay not only for
themselves but for the sons of the rich—for the
fees from the slaves go to pay the daily
lecturers. Yet, it is a strange fact that the
men who work with their hands or their brains
for bread, and afterwards stud}' either for
degrees or purely for information, turn out
better men than those who scoop up the College
scholarships, and fight the wardens for taking
off hot breakfasts at eight o’clock.
Among the numerous aggravations inflicted
upon the rational newspaper reader the current
lengthy reports and comments on charitable
institutions are especially sickening. Every-
body knows that charity is simply a miserable
substitute wrung from the rich for rights
wrongfully withheld. Self-interest, if not
common decency, demands that the monopolists
of land and capital shall periodically dole out
enough to keep the beasts of burden from posi-
tive starvation. We now read that the homes for
the recipients of charity must on no account
remain situated in such prominent positions as
to draw public attention thereto, such social
sores must be concealed. Further, that it is unfair
that the land-owners of property in the neigh-
bourhood of Relief Depots should be denied
their rights to their unearned increments by
the depreciation in value of their allotments.
The feelings of the destitute themselves have
hitherto been cheerfully ignored, yet it certainly
is desirable that a few of these should appear
before the Royal Commission personally and in
their rags and hungry condition to tender their
experiences and offer opinions on these matters
wherein they alone are interested. It is not
cash gratuities that the pauper solicits, it is the
right to obtain access to natural opportunities
whereby he may be enabled to earn his own
subsistence.

Now that the burst of public joy over the col-
lapse of Gillies has subsided, Victorians arc
beginning to consider the impossibility of doing
much better with James Munro. Radical im-
provement in the land laws, or a strict enforce-
ment of the regulations bearing upon building-
societies, &c., are not to be expected from a man
like Jimmy, who has grown old in promoting
companies and grey in grabbing land. Rightly
or wrongly, it has been the custom for years
past to snort at the mention of Muneo’s name.
He is a gentleman who travels along this vale
of tears with a temperance tract in one hand
and a syndicate prospectus in the other —too
much after the fashion of MiramS to be regarded
as a promising Premier. However, there is one
chance for Jimmy Munro to prove himself a
genuine article. Let him spare the country the
expense of loading up the political and “repre-
sentative ” gang with champagne on every pos-
sible excuse. The writer has heard the new Vic-
torian Premier denounce strong drink as the curse
of any country, and is now waiting to hear the
old lamb proclaim that all Government feeds
under his regime will have to be washed down
with lemonade. It is useless for Jimmy to
shout “economy” whilst the Government bill
for “ poison ” amounts to thousands a year, and
if he sanctions the presence of one black bottle
at a Cabinet-meeting his blessed Temperance
principles are outraged by himself, as Treasurer.
Also, if he lures land-sale innocents into buying-
allotments under the influence of bottled beer
there is no health in him. The country has its
eye on Munro. If he goes back upon his
doctrines, and allows the Lady Loch to be used
as a free floating Government pub, and, gener-
ally speaking, carries on like the unredeemed
Gillies, the country will hit him over the head
with a cold water bottle and bid him be gone.
The first duty of a leading public humbug is to
behave as sich.
——
The exploring expedition “to the unknown
parts of Australia,” projected by Sir Thomas
Elder, is likely to excite but the faintest pos-
sible interest outside a very small circle. The
cold, realistic monotony of the Australian in-
terior is pretty well known to everybody, and
the ground about to lie traversed has been
intersected by many routes, leaving nothing but
a few bare geographical details to be tilled in.
The days when Australian exploration awoke
the sympathetic thrill of romantic interest,
throughout the hearts of home-keeping men,
are past. No more will readers of the diary of
a modern explorer feel the same, glow that
penetrated Sturt’s gallant crew when, “ shot
out of the Morumbidgee,” they sat and gazed
in wonder on the broad and noble river
into which their impetuous course had hurried
them. The mystery of the heart of Australia
is one no longer, and the most we can hope to
learn will be the finding of some permanent
water and a few square miles of pastoral
country. The field of the proposed trip is
from Charlotte Waters on the telegraph-
line, in a south-westerly direction, cutting
diagonally the tracks of J. Forrest and E.
Giles, then north-west to the Weld Springs on
Forrest’s track, thence north-east to where
R. C. Gregory lost Sturt’s Creek in a flooded
flat. All this country has been trenched on by
many private parties as well as by the public
expeditions. By filling up the blanks left, the
proposed expedition may do useful, if some-
what tedious, work ; but it will be a mistake to
indulge in any high falutin enthusiasm in the
matter.
The Fire at Boss's Farm.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The squatter saw his pastures wide
Decrease, as one by one
The farmers moving to the west
Selected on his run ;
Selectors took the water up
And wrought the squatter harm —
The richest land upon the flat
Was spoilt by Ross’s Farm.
Full many a scheme to shift old Ross
Had racked the squatter’s brains,
But Ross had got the stubborn blood
Of Scotland in his veins;
He held the land and fenced it in
And cleared and ploughed the soil,
And year by year a richer crop
Repaid him for his toil.
Between the homes for many years
The war did not relax,
For Black impounded Ross's stock,
And Ross impounded Black’s;
A well upon the lower run
Was filled with earth and logs,
And Black laid baits about the farm
To poison Ross’s dogs.
It was, indeed, a deadly feud
Of class and creed and race )
But, yet, there was a Romeo
And Juliet in the case;
And oft at eve across the flats
Beneath the Southern Cross
Young Robert Black was seen to ride
With pretty Jenny Ross.
There came at last a Christmas time,
With fear and ruin dire,
For many miles around the run
The bush was all on fire;
And when the shades of evening fell
The scene was grand and strange —
The hill-fires gleamed like lighted streets
Of cities in the range.
The cattle-tracks between the trees
Were like long crimson aisles,
And on a sudden breeze the fire
Would gallop twenty miles ;
Like sounds of distant musketry
It crackled thro’ the breaks,
And o'er the flats of growing grass
It hissed like angry snakes.
It leapt across the flowing streams
And raced o’er pastures broad,
It climbed the Hees and lit the boughs
And through the scrubs it roared ;
The bees fell stifled in the smoke
Or perished in their hives,
And with the stock the kangaroos
Went flying for their lives.
The sun had set on Christmas Eve,
When, through the scrub-lands wide
Young Robert Ross came riding home
As only natives ride.
He galloped to the homestead door
And shouted in alarm :
“ The fire is past the eastern spur,
And nearing Ross’s farm.
“Now, father, send the men at once,
And evil pay with good ;
Poor Ross’s wheat is all he has
To buy his children food.”
“ Then let it burn,” the squatter said ;
“ I’d like to see it done —
I’d bless the fire if it would clear
Selectors from the run.”
“ Go if you will ” (the squatter said),
“ You shall not take the men—
Go out and join your father’s foes,
And don’t come here again.”
“ I’ll not return,” young Robert cried,
His anger rising higher,
And then he turned his horse’s head
And galloped to the fire.
And there, for three long weary hours,
Half-blind with dust and heat,
Old Ross and Robert fought the flames
That neared the ripened’wheat.
The farmer’s hand was nerved by fears
Of ruin and of loss,
And Robert fought the creeping flames
For love of Jenny Ross.
But inch by inch the stubborn foe
Compelled them to retreat
Until they reached the narrow track
That ran abovei.be wheat ;
‘ The track is now our only hope,
There we must stand,” cried Ross.
“ For nought on earth can stop the flames
If once they get across.”
Then came a cruel gust of wind,
And, with a sudden rush,
The flames leapt o’er the narrow path
And lit the fence of brush.
“The farm must burn !” the farmer cried,
“ We cannot save it now,”
And down upon the blackened ground
He tossed the blackened bough.
But madly, in a rush of hope,
His heart began to beat,
For o’er the crackling fire he heard
The sound < f horses’ feet.
“Here’s help at last ” young Robert cried,
And even as he spoke
The squatter and a dozen men
Came bursting through the smoke.
Down on the ground the stockmen jumped
And bared .each brawny arm,
And tore green branches from the trees
And fought for Ross’s farm ;
And when, before the gallant band,
The beaten flames gave way,
Two grimy hands in friendship joined—
And it was Christmas Day.
HENRY LAWSON.

Apropos to amateurs. At a recent rendition of some
dismal drama in a Northern N.S.W. country town, when
the wounded hero crawled upon the stage with the blood
streaming from the wound which one hand convulsively
pressed, from under that hand fell a ripe prickly pear,
whence the hero had been vigorously squeezing the
gore. And then the audience began to cnioy themselves-
Considering that the Sydney Amateur Operatic Society
made their diibut in “Les Cloches de Corneville” at the
“Standard” last week, your Sappho dips her pen in
raspberry Jam to compliment that numerous aggregation
on the dressing, staging, and grouping of the opera. The
attire of everybody concerned was really good, and if the
chorus looked like as if they had laid on the blush with
a broom, and if some of the gentlemen supes had
forgotten to wad above their ankles, they were only still
more reminiscent of the “ perfeshional ” after all. The
orchestra was good, too, the fiddles only going hopelessly
out of time at the fag-end of the show on the secend
performance ; and regarded from the standpoint of light
and color and row, the opera was quite a success. As for
the principals, Miss Ed. Deane made a lively “ Serpolette,”
and “ Grenicheux ” on the second evening was a daisy
to look at in pink tights and a striped upper garment
like a lolly “ humbug,” hut Mr. Stanley Armstrong as
“Gaspard ” was the twinkling star of the evening, getting
an enthusiastic “ name ” call at the close of the second
act. The proceeds of the show will be handed to the
Girls’ Friendly Society.
If local lady philanthropists gaspingly await an outlet
for their heuevolence t let them go round with a cookery-
hook, not a flower-pot, and teach ignorant, thriftless
wives how to boil a potato into neither wax nor smash,
and how to make attractive cheap dishes in place of the
watery “stoo” and half-burnt chops that for ever indi-
gestibly adorn the bill of fare. It may be objected that
the ministoring angels don’t know how, any more than
their victims, in this case, but that objection can soon be
overcome in the case of anyone not unwilling to soil her
hands. But there ! what is the use of advocating utility
against beauty? It is far nicer to be met going down the
Block with a charity smile and a basket of roses than to
he hidden away in a squalid kitchen teaching some untidy
creature how to make curry.
The all-pervading cookery-class is beginning to be eyed
askance by the legislator who has anything to do with
public schools. At almost every spree in connection with
these the legend runs—“ Refreshments, prepared by the
School cookery classes, were served,” and one can imagine
the sinking feelings of the martyr member when the
glassy potato, the coddled turkey, the soggy sponge-coke,
and the flabby jelly of maiden effort become eternal
features in the festivities. And who can blame him?
Everybody gushes about the advantages of cookery
classes, and everybody leaves the “ proof of the pudding ”
to, 'say, his mother-in-law.
Lady Carington has gone ; vanished tearfully into the
beyond, as it were, hut ere she went she advocated a
scheme which, if carried out, will lead to steel boiler-
shape hats and iron umbrellas being the only local wear.
A gushing article in S.M.H. explains things. It says:—
Lady Carington herself, in a last request, has answered
us. When forwarding her donation for a horticultural
prize she has asked that, if po<sihlc, that prize shall he
given, wholly or in part, for window-gardening in the city
of R.i dney.
Upon which the “Lily Window-Gardening Association”
is suggested, the members of which shall go round
amidst the top stories with death-dealing pots full of
double geraniums, sunflowers, and other cheap and top-
heavy ebullitions of nature, which they will place on
the window-sill of Poverty, with instructions to carefully
weed and water. No doubt a flower on a city window-
sill - especially the window-sill of a God-forsaken city like
London—has a certain artistic influence, hut in the public
interest, your Sappho hastily submits that the association
ought not to he encouraged. Flower-pots on the sills are
very poetical until you receive one on the cerebrum, and
the facility with which they tumble 100 ft. and block a
tall hat, quite a foot out of the vertical line of descent,
make them a dangerous method of elevating the
“ masses.” In p aris, sill flower-pots were at one time
suppressed, because they were handy weapons of offence ;
and the temptation to drop one on to the head of an
enemy or a “pleeceman” might prove too much for the
Sydney slums.

you can’t help yearning for something to yawn behind

“ Frou Frou ” was staged in a sudden, fitful way at
Sydney Royal on 29th ult., but the Potter had time to
ransack her wardrobe and come forth in special glories in
the middle acts of the pathetic picture of feminine frailty
though why she didn’t dress Watt-Tanner too isn’t
apparent. In the second act —the act where the willowy
Gilberte hangs herself over a chair-back and laughs a
hoarse, deep, unexpected cackle that makes your flesh
creep—her gown caused a well-known artist who sat
behind me breathing whisky on the back of my neck to
give a deep, soulful “ ah,” expressive of soothed suscepti-
bilities. The trained over-dress and elbow-sleeves were
of crushed strawberry watered velvet over an under gar-
ment of flesh-pink silk veiled in white net bordered with
gold galon. In the second part of the act a somewhat
paper-pattern garment is worn in pink silk covered with
pink tulle and touches of pink passementerie, balloon
sleeves of Spring green gauze powdered in gold spangles,
yellow velvet opera-cloak bordered with white swansdown.
In this act the Countess de Cambri (Emma G wynne) wore
a neat visiting dress of apricot-tweed with Figaro bodice
and shawl pattern design. In the third act Mrs. Potter
wore a beautiful dress—the bodice and train of white
brocade with white velvet stripe; jupe and lace, the
waist bound about with a yellow silk scarf; tight fitting
white sleeves with loops of yellow ribbon from shoulder
to waist; the shoulders having epaulettes of brown fur, an
arrangement of old gold lace about the neck and a girdle
of gold cord and tassels finishing off the splendor very
effectively. In the fourth act the leading lady wore a
cinnamon-brown woollen gown, with puffs round the
elbows and shoulder gables of brown velveteen £cru
chiffon fichu and lace to her knuckles, and waist-spanner
of brown and gold guipure braid. But why does the
Brown-Potter grasp her petticoats in front and haul them
almost to her knees when she pursues anybody across the
stage? This skirtal eccentricity is only permissible when
the patient has No. 2 feet and is negotiating a mud-
puddle.
Miss Myra Kemble has leased a house in Macquarie-
street North, Sydney—No. 365. The furnishing is in the
hands of a well-known Sydney firm, who expect to have
things “ homey” in a very short time. Myra’s admirers
are mostly responsible for the bric-a-brac. It is a great and
economical thing to be a public idol, isn’t it?
Lieutenant-Governor Sir Alfred Stephen, with his
family, will shortly vacate hot and dusty Sydney for the
shades of Sutton Forest. They will occupy the vice-regal
residence, of course. When you come to look at it, it is
a great and economical thing to be a Lieutenant-Governor,
too.
And, apropos to things gubernatorial—the wife of a
Sydney Parliamentary Minister has received a gushing
letter from Lady Carington, mostly referring to the
children, and saying that those pale-haired, pale-faced,
well-behaved little solemnities ceased to encore their
meals after leaving Adelaide, and that the party generally
were experiencing a delightful feeling of 1 oing off the
chain for the first time in five weary years Only, of
course, Lady C. did not express it in that way. :•
The death of Mrs. Morehead, wife of lion. B. D. More-
head, ex Premier of Queensland, which occurred at the
Royal Hotel, Sydney, on the 28th inst, seems doubly sod,
happening, as it did, at the outset of a holiday trip to
Europe, for which berths had been secured on the Aus-
tral. Mrs. Morehead intended paying a visit to her
mother in the country before leaving Australia, and was
to have set out on the day she died. The family will
return to Queensland.
The oily Believer who sat in his pew chuckling over his
“ hand.” in re “Carbine,” and meekly putting threepence
in the plate held out by the devotional deacon whose
coal-shares were steadily going up, must have blushed ail
over his bald head when the “ officiating priest” read the
Pastoral letter of the Primate, on a recent Sunday. For
Bishop Smith (who is no relation of mine, remember,)
simply begged for cash. “The (Anglican) Church,” he
said, “ needs more systematic support. . . . The
income was £650 less than the receipts of last year.”
Which his Grace attributed blandly and blindly to “ the
wet;” “absence of offertories at confirmation services
“the late strike”—to anything hut the real reason—viz.,
that the gate is poor because the houses are thin. The
men don’t go to church at all, being utterly indifferent
to the voice of a class which is not sufficiently virile to
command their respect; and, as to the women, many of
whom do attend church, for one reason or other, they are
too close-fisted to help the plate much, and so devote
their very smallest change to the spread of the Glad
Tidings.
The fact of the Sydney Women’s Literary Club holding
their inaugural “conversazione” on the evening of the
day on which the late Professor Stephens was interred,
could not fail to cast a shadow over the affair, and
several guests omitted to attend under the impression of
a postponement, since the Club is somewhat of a ’Varsity
interest. All the wits stopped at home, evidently, and
the proceedings (the circumscribed Temperance Hall was
the scene of action) were faintly suggestive of a ’samina-
tion in a suburban school. Though hilled as a
“conversazione,” the entertainment included gaspy
recitations, pianoforte soli, warhlings, and a very
moderate amount of “ conversazione” of the elementary
sort, when your adversary asks you a question and you
endeavour to answer it and feel deeply grateful, when the
company say, “Hus-s-h!” and a lanky youth arises to
melodise and save you from further mental wear and
tear, till the next awful gap. But though the soiree was
such a frost, it is soothing to know that the Club was the
first to recognise the symptoms. Indeed, one despondent
member took another despondent member on one sid e
and remarked that it was “enough to DO for us.”

the ladies of Sydney to pay off the liabilities of the
non-paying orchestral concert season just concluded, A
billed to happen at the Centennial Hall on 11th inst. The

M. Chaperon, a noted vigneron of Minto, recently gave
a dinner-party and ball in honour of his niece’s birthday
—Mademoiselle Eva Chaperon is a noted beauty— which
was attended by all the elite of the neighbourhood.
I hear that at a certain ladies’ club lately, when “ Her
Excellency ” sent a return letter of thanks in reply to one
written by the club’s secretary, such a “ pounce ” took
place for the holy writ that it was torn into bits in a
second, each bandit seizing all she could, while the genius
who grabbed the autograph—one of the private secre-
tary’s most successful official forgeries—actually danced
a polka on the sofa. Which somehow reminds me of th e
lover who was wont to kiss the backs of the stamps on his
billets-doux because she had licked them, until one day
he saw her give the letter to the nigger to stamp, when
ail his young enthusiasm fell of a heap.

The curtain finally dropped upon the
closing scene of the Newcastle cause cilehre Martin
v. Arnott, on Saturday night last, when the com-
mittee decided to hand over the money that
had been collected for the deluded maiden from the
public to the girl herself, which, with the £3OO
already given her by certain friends, makes a
handsome compensation, so far as money can com-
pensate, for one false step. Mr. J. A. Gorrick,
the solicitor who so ably conducted the case for the

mittee. The local press, as usual, keeps a discreet
silence on all matters affecting the morals of lead-
ing residents.
From the central Queensland distilleries
there flows a perennial stream of rum, for which
there is an enormous local and export demand.
Within four weeks of it leaving the still this tan-
glefoot is retailed across the bars of Bundaberg,
Brisbane, and many minor centres, besides being
shipped wholesale on the “ blackbirders ” which
“recruit ” the niggers in the South Pacific. It is
this or a similar product which drives so many of
the Government Agents mad, and which is re-
sponsible for so many of the frequently-reported
bloodthirsty outrages of the South Sea Islands.
Since The Bulletin mocked at Melbourne
Age for printing such miserable cant as “we
cannot be asked to seriously believe that
Parnellism still remains enshrined in the hearts
of its devotees,” that “radical,” broad-minded
pennyworth has made a feeble attempt to despise
its own hypocricy. The leader in last Saturday's
Age (the remark applies equally to the leader in
last Saturday's Augus) might have been written
by a man who had previously swallowed The
Bulletin's “Plain English ” ideas on “ Parnell-
ism and Divorce,” and found it good food for the
brain. “At present,” said the second Age
article, “the cases of Sir Charles Dilke and Mr.
Parnell show conclusively that no man who could
not have gone in quest of the Holy Grail will
be allowed fo serve his country in high office. . .
If the Irish case for Home Rule had any value six
weeks ago it sin ely ought not to be sacrificed to
the consideration that one indispensable man has
been fatally frail’’—fatally meaning that he had
been found out. Anon the Age (like the
Augus, again) goes on to remark that Nelson
was giddy, and the Royal Family has had its
lapses—in short, the Syme oracle practically
supplies a strong antidote to its poisonous rot
of the previous week. But neither the Age nor
Augus comments upon the fact that it has devoted
a column or so to writing itself down one of two
things—a rash ass or a designing humbug.

The popularity which Mrs. Potter has
attained with a section of her Australian audiences
was mainly built up by her appearance in new
plays and her avoidance of characters recently
impersonated by capable performers. Now her
theatrical reputation, sadly shattered by the
ineptitude of her Kate Hardcastle, has been
wholly undermined by her attempt to handle the
variformity of Frou-Frou. Never were her broad
enunciation, coarse intonation, lack of repose
chin-jerks, and angular movements more irrita-
ting y in evidence. The authors’ Gilberte is, at
all times, a lady, but in the hands of the Potter
she became hoydenish when she should have been
arch, affected, self-conscious, and giddy when she
should have been bashful or modest, rude
when she should have been indifferent, im-
pudent when she should have been con-
temptuous, and simply noisy when power
and passion were required. Kyrle Bellew’s
Sartorys did not come well out of the com-
parison with that of Titheradge, and was,
without doubt, bis worst Sydney perform-
ance. He dressed the part badly, altogether
failed to give it the weight, dignity and polish
which it demands, and was only once effective—
that when he confronts his faithless wife in her
Venetian retreat. Moreover, his gait, reminiscent
of the strut of a feather-legged, long-spurred
rooster, though not displeasing when displayed in
classic or mediaeval costume, is distinctly at
variance with modern garb. All the other parts,
especially Louise, Brigard and De Valreas, were ill-
cast and abominably played. Even when the brief-
ness of rehearsal and unsuitability of Sydney Royal
for the representation of sucha play werecqnsidered
as palliatives, the whole show was so feeble that
even the critics who had hitherto most stoutly
stuck to P. B. k Co. were compelled to be mildly
reproachful. Yet the S.M. Herald, a paper that
has stood by Mrs. Potter through good and evil
report, out-slobbered all its previous slobberings
by devoting the best part of a column to her un-
qualified glory. The tenor of it may be imagined
from the following quotation : “ Her Frou Frou
is entitled to rank with that of Sara Bernhardt,
and in some particulars it is a better performance
than that of the great French actress.” Whew!
To make matters worse, the version of Meilhac
and Halevy’s play was the worst that has
ever come under our notice. Bold assertion had
taken the place of delicate suggestion, some of the
situations were weakened and several of the
speeches lost significance by transference to other
characters than those originally designed to give
them utterance. “David Garrick ” was revived on
Tuesday night, “She Stoops” on Wednesday, and
“ La Tosca ” will be played to-night (Thursday)
and to-morrow night. The two stars then pro-
ceed to give a series of dramatic recitals at New-
castle, Maitland, Ballarat and Adelaide ere de-
parting for India accompanied by the faithful
Twinning. Then they intend to produce “Hero
and Leander” in London. “Down went
M’Ginty,” &c.

When the New Garden Theatre (N.Y.)
was opened with “Dr. Bill,” the seats were
auctioned for £ISOO. Judging from later reports,
however, the piece has not been so successful
there as at Sydney Criterion, where the business
of last week was the largest ever done in the
house, excepting during one week of the Essie
Jenyns’ season, which was supplemented by a
matinee. The somewhat dull ending has been lit
up by the rc-entrance of Patfie Brown still
afflicted by St. Vitus, and the curtain now falls
while the entire company are doing the kangaroo-
dance. But it is the spiciness of the second act
that fetches them; especially the highly-
suggestive situation where the young dude is
locked-upin a dark bedroom, as he thinks with a
mash, but in reality with a friend's wife, who
takes him for her husband, who is locked-up in a
shower-bath with his damp mother-in-law, while
the supposed mash and the fatlier-in-law are
fishing-np the keys of the locked doors from the
depths of their spinal regions, where they had
been plunged as a cure for fainting fits and
apoplexy. With “5.R.0.” at every entrance,
even on Monday evening, “Dr. Bill” looks like
running up to Christmas. Then, it is said, the
fair Myra intends touring with it. Wo should
advise her to be careful. Ansons, Taylors, Wards,
Browns and Broughs don’t grow on gum-trees;
and in less capable hands the eccentric medico
might prove a bitter pill.

“ The Merry Wives of Windsor ” is doing
good business at Sydney Her Majesty’s. The
wives are perfectly safe in the hands of Kate
Bishop and Watts-Phillips, and the swash-
bucklerism of Pistol, the fiery-nosed alcoholism of
Bardolph, and the spiteful rascality of Nym are
cleverly depicted by Messrs. Sweeney, Whyte, and
our old friend Burford. The old procuress and go-
between, Mistress Quickly, is played brightly,
albeit she looks over youthful, by Mabel Tracey.
Joe Tola.no, as Rugby, makes good bricks without
straw. The host of A. Rigby is full-voiced,
round bellied, and altogether satisfactory, and
Bessie Rignold and Arthur Greenaway have
nought to do but look beautiful—an end to which
they were specially constructed —as the lovers,
Anne and Fenton. But “Bill” Rignold is the
Alpha and Omega, the top, bottom and sides of
the production.

The most effectively tragic incident of the
P. B. season took place on the last night of “ Hero
and Leander,” which classic nudity was with-
drawn prior to previous announcements in order
to allow of the staging of “ Camille ” on Friday.
The principals had just retired, after taking a call
at the close of the first act, when John Payne, a
25-year-old jack-tar from H.M.S. Orlando —who
had apparently come to the theatre principally to
reproach one of the Paphian sisterhood, who, it is
alleged, had jilted him after borrowing all his
earnings, £35 —bounded from the fifth row of stalls,
over the orchestra and footlights, on to the stage.
Having attained that evil eminence, he quickly
hauled out a derringer, thegiftof the faithless fair,
shot himself in the pit of the stomach and foil in
most picturesque fashion on the prompt side of
the stage. Only a tiny report was heard, a small
puff of smoke seen in the auditorium, and most
of the audience thought Payne’s fireworks were
part of the play, until his mate clambered on the
stage air! removed him with the assistance of

The Italian residents of Australia have
formed a league and appealed to their Government
to make arrangements such as will prevent Italian
single men from being engaged for Queensland
sugar-plantations at low wages.
Mr. J. H. Hunt, artist, of Norwich
Chambers, Sydney, has just completed a half-
length, life-size portrait of that mild and amiable
veteran of diplomacy, Sir John Robertson. It is
suggested that the design for the frame should be
of champagne bottles, separated by swear-words,
picked out with Cabbage Garden ornamentation.

Despite an effort made in the N.S.W.
Assembly to compel him to eat, Jacques Ibbot,
the starving bag of bones from Bondi, now too
weak to stand on his feet, may be expected to
conclude his fast this (Thursday) evening, when
a big crowd will attend to see him partake of his
first meal. Manager Wyburd has, we are in-
formed, taken a lease of the premises, and his
next novelty will be, it is said, Capt. Jorgensen’s
ocean-boat, the Storm King.

The first social evening of the Coogee
Palace Aquarium Club will be held in the
grounds at Coogee on Friday night, when special
trams will be run to and from the grounds. The
innovation is likely to be a big draw on warm
summer nights. On Saturday, Mr. Corbett, the
swimmer, will be accorded a benefit, when a big
programme will be gone through. In future,
every Wednesday night will be devoted to dancing.

Boucicault’s sporting comedy, “ The Jilt”
—handsomely mounted, and for the most part ex-
cellently played at Melbourne Bijou —shows the
author at his best and worst. Its main reef of
serious interest gets pinched out, and its charming
comedy conceits are associated with sporting
notions so preposterous that the story, leaving its
last vestige of reality upon an impossible race-
course, loses all claim to sober consideration. A
little more attention to petty detail might have
won for “ The Jilt ” the popularity which Bouci-
cault expected for this literary pet of his declining
years. It failed because he chose to flaunt his
ignorance of racing in the face of the public,
whilst at tire same time professing to appeal to
their intelligence with a high-class play. “Flying
Scud,” which was at least sensational in its
absurdity, didn't challenge the test of technical
criticism, but “The Jilt” cannot avoid the
necessity of being weighed in the sporting balance
and found wanting. Even before the farcical
“Marquee—Overlooking the Racecourse”
scene is arrived at sundry laws and customs of the
turfy world are recklessly outraged. A, gentle-
man jockey and part-owner of an intended
winner of the Liverpool Grand National
goes prowling round country houses with
his racehorse at his heels, because he can’t
trust it out of his care. And this “dead bird,”
which he fixes up at the nearest pub. whilst he
goes mashing an heiress, is supposed to be in full
tr lining all the time. Another stroke of insanity
takes the form of a racing commissioner and
money-lender, who carries business cards inscribed
with announcements like “odd*given or taken.”
It certainly appears that this genius is a “ broker, ”
but the author foolishly represents him as a downy
blackleg, instead of a callow amateur. An old
lady horsetrainer is possible enough, but the idea
of her daughter winning a steeplechase (the Grand
National, presumably) in her ordinary riding-
habit is as monstrous as the actual spectacle of
owner and trainer seeing the race from a distant
tent, where two jockeys in their silks have been
making love until the race is about to start. Yet
“The Jilt ” must be taken seriously, or not at all.
It is at times pathetic and often brilliantly-humor-
ous. It shows how badly a great writer needed an
increase of his store of knowledge by conversing
with a stable-boy for ten minutes.

Seccombo, who gives one but a very faint im-
pression of a girl likely to win the Grand
National, over four miles of rough going,

There was a. good house for ‘ ‘Arrah-na-Pogue”
at Melbourne Royal, and the florid virtues of the
legitimate Milesian drama were recognised in the
usual manner. Coming after a thin cockney yarn
like “The English Rose,” which appealed neither
to the head nor the heart, this thirty-year-old
favorite is very welcome. It glows with the
warmth of stage Irish life, wherein patriotic
griefs are liberally alleviated by whisky, and a
distressful pisantry endorse the assertion that
theirs is the most downtrodden country under the
sun by dancing a jig. When the hero is plunged
into prison he plunges out again; a proscribed rebel
who hides around in hay-lofts for a time, ultimately
gets a free pardon, and altogether the ir n rule of
the perfidious Saxon is made to,appear a fairly joyous
nis itution. No now terms of praise can be found
tor Maggie Moore and Grattan Riggs in such parts
as those of .Shaun and Arrah. and it is necessary
only to mention that the former sings her two
songs with all the old effect. Olyndes is racy of
the soil as Colonel O Grady, and Royce does his
duty by Michael Feeney. Minor parts are in safe
hands, too, but the acting of the recent arrivals —
Harold Russell and Miss Kingsley —is rather short
vveight in both cases. Talking of the importa-
tions, Laurence Cautley is suffering from our
glorious Australian climate, and his place in the
“Rose” had to be filled last week by George
Melville, who pulled through steadily.
{* * * *
“ Boccaccio,” at the Opera House, has
held its own against the elements as well as could
be expected, but no better. Most nights last week
the voice of the tempest was trying its level best
to shut down the prima-donna, and the funny
men had to do all they knew to keep fresh wheezes
off the chest with an umbrella. It was a cold
time for shows entirely. Provided that the gentle
dew from Heaven has left off descending in
bucketsful and the hurricane has consented to
listen to reason, “ Boccaccio ” should fetch the
multitude until Saturday, when “Madame Angot”
takes up the bill. By the way, the shallow Mac-
Mahonian device of sticking, at a late hour,
“ Full ” boards outside the theatre (when it wasn’t
full at all) is not being adopted by the present
management.
*» * *
“For Love and Life,” the original hypno-
tic drama by Messrs. Dam pier and Walch, tried
its mysterious influence upon the Alexandra on
Saturday night, when the gallery boys certainly
betrayed symptoms of mesmeric sleepiness. The
story is long-winded and unsympathetic. Dam-
pier, the ostensible hero, appears to let a mes-
merist reduce him to the miserable condition of a
“subject,” in consideration of the sum of one
pound sterling, paid in advance. The mesmerist
is actuated by a pure love of science, but the gal-
lant hero goes straight out for the twenty shillings,
and is no doubt loaded up with castor-oil and
things in an adjoining room before he comes
wandering across a murder in his trance. Then
he seems to walk home, with his brain dazed and
his hat dented in, remarking that it all seems like
a dream. In the unnatural course of things this
drowsy Juggins thinks he committed the murder
which the audience know to have been perpetrated
by a dark man with a baleful glare in both eyes.
Nobody cares for the poor “subject’s” agony of
mind, and when he is mesmerised back to con-
sciousness of his own innocence, and the other
man gets scruffed by a thick-tongued detective in
a summer suit, it is a matter of no importance.
The drama neither teaches nor entertains, and its
construction is not so tricky as other Damperian
efforts. F. C. Appleton represents a mesmerist in
a far more taking style than Kennedy did, while
Dampier is doubtless a conscientious and therefore
tedious exponent of a decidedly weak “subject.”
Further blessings to follow if the play catches on.

The Melbourne fasting-man has put in a
cheerful first week at the Waxworks, and looks
forward to spending a merry Christmas on warm
greetings and cold water. At present the weather
remains favourable. When it gets hot, and the
typhoidy drains trickle into the Yan Yean, the
chances are that Felix Tanner will feel strange
events transpiring within. The music-halls and
etceteras are getting through the dull season
creditably, and Madame Patey’s perseverance
was rewarded by a noble attendance on Saturday
night. The season is supposed to conclude with a
couple of oratorio performances next week, but,
no doubt, there will be another ballad concert in
the meantime, or as a wind-up.
** * *
The salient features of the Sydney Operatic
Society’s performance of “ Les Cloches de Corne-
ville” were the clean limbedness of the male
chorus, and the full bust-edness of the female ditto.
The choral singing was fresh and full, but- lacking
in light and shade, and the orchestra in most
cases drowned the solo-singers and invariably
galloped them off their vocal feet. The
Serpolette of Miss Deane (Syd. Deane’s
sister) was after the manner of Nellie Stewart.
The lady has a nice, little voice, that wouldn’t
stand much of the wear and tear of comic opera.
The Bailie and Gobo of Messrs. Allen and Carr
were very good, and the Gaspard of S. Arm-
stx-ong, otherwise feeble, was effective in the
gold scene. Miss Alice Galvin was a chipper
cabin-boy. The opera was fairly well-staged and
brightly dressed.
At overty Point.
Reference to old Paddy Belfleld recalls the yarn trage-
dian “Bill” Ryan (now growing roses and pumpkins at
Abbotsford, Vic.) tells of his first engagement in Aus-
tralia. Belfleld at that time (1855) was running the
Sydney Lyceum, and was engaging people for a long
season of melodrama, opening with the blood-curdling
“Dream at Sea.” By and bye the manager came to
Ryan (whose real name is Arrowsmith, and who had just
then arrived from the old country). “All right,” said
Paddy, when he had fixed terms, &c., “you play the
juvenile—that’ll do, and what did you say your name
was?” “William Arrowsmith,” answered the new man.
Paddy snorted disgustedly. “That won’t do at all,” he
said ; “you'll be playing to Irishmen most. All my other
people have got Irish names and you’ll have to take an
Irish name, too. We’ll call you Ryan.” With the lament-
able result that William Arrowsmith has been known as
“ Bill Ryan ” ever since.

such as one sees at any of the popular dramatic theatres,
Melbourne Alexandra for instance, revolts at, surelv it is
the nauseating allusions to “ The Queen ” as the secret of
England’s alleged greatness, made at leastonce in every im-
ported sensational play. But the bed-rock of moral
degradation has been reached by the author of “New
Babylon.” During the recent revival of this otherwise
good old treasury-filler, Teddy Ryan as Ah Luck, the
Chinese servant, has to deliver several lick-spittling
remarks in regard to Mrs. Guelph. Ramirez, it will be
remembered, enters disguised as an Italian, hawking

is worsted. In revenge he destroys all the Italian’s stock-
in-trade, throwing Garibaldi and the Bather off at the
wings But when he comes to a bust of her Gracious,
instead of saying “ Too muchee no good,” or something of
that sort, he kisses it fondly on the nose, and remarks,

John Chinaman,” and still kissing it he trots off. And a

who married the great English contralto, Charlotte
Dolby, whose sister, Mrs. Pedley, is resident in Sydney,
died recently' in England of bronchial asthma. He

Fred, de Belleville, well-known in Australians rehearsing
“ Man and Woman ” at Proctor’s Theatre, N. Y.
The well-known Danish Herbalist, Herr
Rasmussen, certainly scored a point when he pur-
chased that property now known as the Mount
Lizzie Orchard and Vineyard, situated within
one and a half miles from Minto. Four years ago
this land was only partially cleared, and its im-
provement in so short a time makes it evident
that no expense has been spared in order to make
it a model estate. The temporary dwelling is
situated on the summit of a hill, so that an un-
broken view of the Flue Mountains is secured.
The concert-hall, fernery, almond-groves, foun-
tains and wine-cellars are worthy of note. A
new house is about to be erected at a cost of £2OOO,
which will be quite a novelty in that district.
Herr Rasmussen has, in all, twelve hundred
full-bearing fruit trees and 3000 vines. One por-
tion of the land is devoted entirely to the cultiva-
tion of the herbs used in his profession. He
is an experienced botanist, and fully intends to
import and propagate many plants useful in
medical science hitherto not grown in this
country.*
At next N.S. W, general election, the New-
castle and district labor bodies, who make nosecret of
their intention of getting level with their enemies
at the ballot boxes, hope to be so effectively
organised as to be able, for the first time in history,
to cast every vote for their own candidates. A
Labor League has been formed, which all union
lodges will join, and from the lodge-delegates will
be formed a Central Parliamentary Committee,
whose duty it will be to lay down a Labor plat-
form, to the principles of which Labor candidates
must subscribe, signing a bond to resign their
seats if called upon to do so consequent on de-
parture from the principles upon which they were
elected. Three candidates must be selected by
ballot by the central committee, and these candi-
dates all unions will be called upon to support at
the poll. The unions will find all nomination
money and pay all election expenses, so that per-
sonal poverty shall be no bar to a faithful repre-
sentative of the people’s cause in Parliament.
The unionists don’t care one brass farthing who
puts up on the accustomed lines or who does not.
Let such candidates get what support they may
from outside sources, the Labor votes (to the tune
of many thousands) will go straight for their own
men only. Which looks like real business.
