A Strange Remembrance
(A BULLETIN BUSH SKETCH.).
A scene of utter desolation. Underfoot nothing
but loose slate shingles, not a blade of grass, not a
green herb anywhere. All around stunted nmlga
bushes, grey, sombre, and lifeless. In tiie back-
ground frowning ranges, their rugged slopes
clothed with the same dull desert scrub. A
depressing landscape to gaze upon, especially to
a solitary man fifteen miles from camp with a
tired and footsore horse. . . . ,
The man in question stood beside his weaneci
steed, with his arms resting upon his horse s
withers, j gazing moodily across at the sinking
sun. He had lately taken up and stocked some
country in the northern portion of Western Aus-
tralia; had been following the tracks of a few
head of cattle that had .been frightened by the
blacks, and making a short cut back across the
range his horse had bruised its foot and fallen
lame.
“Nothing for it, old man,” he said, addressing
his dumb companion, “you must try and hobble
on a bit further and perhaps we’ll find a little
grass lower down the gully. No such luck as
water I’m afraid. ” .
Linking his arm through the reins he walked
on, his horse limping painfully after him.
In about half a mile he came to a small opening
in the dark monotony of the mulga scrub, and, as
he was still on a spur of the range he could see the
lower and level country spread beneath him.
The sun was sinking behind an opposing,range of
hills on the other side of the valley. _ Far, far
away to the south he could see the twin-peaked
hill near which was his camp, and down the centre
of the valley could trace the taller foliage that
indicated the course of the river.
It was a lonely outlook. Even as he gazed at
it the sun sank behind the western range, a
shadow passed over the tree tops, turning them, if
possible to a more sombre tint, and a low breath
of wind stole through the surrounding scrub with
an almost human sigh.
Jack Turner was casting another long glance
around before recommencing his tramp when his
attention was attracted by the white trunk of a
gum tree showing out above the scrub only a
short distance away. It was a little off his
homeward track, and as he looked at it curiously,
wondering whether it would be worth while to
make for the spot on the chance of finding a sandy
patch of ground with a little grass for his horse,
he saw, to his astonishment, a puff of smoke arise
and vanish in the air.
“Blacks, of course,” was his thought. Outside
of his camp he knew himself to be the only white
man in all the miles of country now fast dis-
appearing from his gaze in the short-lived
twilight; and a dreary thing it was to know. It
was not the tribe who had been amongst the
cattle, for they had been followed on to another
river and would scarcely venture back for some
months. Most probably it was one of the small,
wandering families, consisting of, perhaps, an
old man, his gin, and a couple of gaunt, sore-
eyed piccanninies, who fought for a half-starved
existence on grubs arid roots in the desert. Who
it was he intended to ascertain, so setting
his course by the bright and beautiful evening
star now descending in the track of the sun, ho
strode on through the darkening shadows until
the smell of burning wood told him lie was nearing
his destination. Approaching cautiously he soon
got sight of the fire, and hanging his horse up to
a tree stole forward, revolver in hand,
A bit of bark blazed up and showed him who
the wanderers were. A withered old gin and a
half-grown child were seated on a tattered skin
beside the fire.
With a momentary feeling of shame, he hastily
replaced his pistol and strode forward, holding
both hands up as a sign of peace that most natives
understand.
With a shrill cry the child, a young girl, sprang
up and, frantic with terror, tried to pull the old
crone on to her feet. She only succeeded in doing
so by the time that Turner was close to them, and
it struck him as strange that they had not run
away into the darkness. The old woman stood
tottering, trying to support herself by a stick,
confused and frightened by the exclamations of
the girl. Then Turner saw that she was blind.
By dint of some trouble he managed to pacify
the girl, and when she found that she was not to
be killed and eaten she became silent, and the
old woman sat down again. Turner threw some
twigs on the fire, for the night had now closed in,
and, as they blazed up, looked around.
The fire was at the foot of the gum-tree that had
attracted his attention. Apparently it was on a
small, sandy flat with a fairly good supply of
grass; at the foot of the tree, which was an old
shell, he could see a circular hole, a native well,
and near the fire stood a good-sized coolamen
nearly full of water. Here was a stroke of luck,
grass and water; and a soft spot to lie down on,
when he had anticipated a stony couch under a
mulga-tree with his poor tired horse tied up to
keen him from futile wandering.
The old dame was safe anchored and the child's
fright was apparently appeased, so he went back
and brought up his horse and unsaddled him.
Then taking his quart-pot he made signs to the
girl that he wanted water. This was at once
understood and seemed to place them on a friendly
footing; taking the quart-pot she went to the hole,
and to Turner’s surprise seemed to disappear in
the earth. A black hand, however, soon appeared
holding the brimming quart aloft full of cool
sweet water, and Turner took it, and indulged in
a long refreshing drink. This reminded him that
poor old Nero, his horse, stood in need of liquid
refreshment, and he examined the well and found
that it had been sunk down nearly five feet, and
that there was a good supply of water at the
bottom. By aid of the' coolamen, out of which
Nero, after many sniffs and snorts, consented to
drink, Turner gave his horse a satisfactory
quencher, the girl filling the quart and handing
it up to him. The ice once broken, Nero finished
off several brimming measures, and then retired
to crop the welcome grass and rest his bruised
frog on the sand.
Turner unstrapped his blankets, and having still
some tea and sugar and bread and meat left, pro-
ceeded to prepare his own meal. After some
difficulty he persuaded his young hostess to taste
some sugar, which was highly appreciated, as was
also some bread. Unfortunately the stock of
provisions was limited. Next morning it was
evident that Nero could not proceed another mile,
go there was nothing for it but to leave him at the
well. There being now a perfect understanding
between the child and himself, Turner explained
that he would be back by the time the sun had
risen and set twice, that she was to give the horse
so many coolamens full of water twice a day until
he returned; all of which she seemed most
intelligently to understand and appreciate. Before
leaving he made her water Nero without his help,
which she did most satisfactorily, and then giving
them his blankets and what bread and meat
was left and, putting his saddle up in the fork of
a tree, he started off for camp, which he reached,
rather tired, that evening, without further adven-
ture.
Next day was Christmas day, and he was easily
persuaded to spell in camp and defer the recovery
of Nero for another day. Having lauded the in-
telligence displayed by the young savage and ex-
pressed his intention of providing her and her old
charge with a tremendous feed in return for their
hospitality, his partner naturally was sufficiently
interested to accompany him when he started back
to the well.
It was mid-day when they reached the neigh-
bourhood of the tree.
“I hope old Nero isn’t dead,” said Turner’s
partner, pointing to two or three crows who, at
their approach, rose up from the scrub and flew
cawing away.
They rode on to the flat and both men pulled up
in surprise. The place was dotted all over with
the remains of fires, fresh tracks covered the
ground everywhere, and it was evident that a
large body of blacks had only just vacated the
camp.
“ you saw nothing of this?” said Turner’s
partner,- Turner shook his head, he could have
sworn that beyond the two representatives lie had
interviewed there was not a nigger within fifty
miles.
jya t or>e j 1 v I
They rode over the deserted camp. ‘ Look a g
the ovens,” said Turner pointing to the remain
of the earthen mounds that had been used for
cooking something bigger than their usual fare. A
light broke on him —“ Poor old Nero !
They had not far to look ; bis hoofs, some horse
hair, and scorched pieces of hide, bore witness to
his fate. Nero had formed the Christmas dinner.
There was nothing to do but ride home again.
The saddle had been hacked to pieces, and the
blacks had more than 24 hours’ start.
Turner was naturally rather aggrieved when he
considered that if he had gone on that night,
walking slowly, he would probably have got his
horse half-way to the camp and saved his life;
but a remark” of his partner’s reconciled him to
the existing state of things. “ Look here, old
man, your luck was dead in. If those bucks had
come along the night you were there instead of
the next you would have been served up for their
Christmas dinner as well as Nero.”
a Which was quite true.
DELCOMYN.
The Victorian Railway Department is 50
per cent, out of pocket on their three ship-loads
of Japanese coal ordered during the strike ! In
other words, they have to pay double the price of
Newcastle coal for a fuel greatly inferior in
quality. When ordering, no samples were for-
warded whereupon to base an agreement. I his
only further impresses upon the public the convic-
tion that the cost of the strike will be borne by
itself all round. In the future, perhaps instead
of looking on apathetically with its hands in its
pockets and a far-away look in its eye, the com-
munity may ([interest itself in the ethical and
economic rights of labour-disputes, , instead of
leaving the conduct of its affairs in the hands of a
batch of callous representatives whose private
sympathies and personal interests are in direct
antagonism to those of their constituents.
The “Kreutzer Sonata."
Dear Bulletin, —When Gilbert published his
revised and condensed “Hamlet” he viewed the
question of that sombre - orphan’s mental condition
from a new standpoint, and speculated as to
whether he was idiotically sane, or sanely idiotic,
or whether he was a person who had broken down
in the effort to be an idiot; and finally he re-
solved that the Danish prince was a fool in his
right mind with occasional lucid intervals of
brilliant derangement. The same argument might
reasonably apply to the incomprehensible Russian
whose “ Kreutzer Sonata ” is the literary sensation
of the moment in Australia, but in his case the
balance of evidence seems to show that he has
made a noble effort to be a lunatic, and has failed
miserably in the attempt. The collapse is hope-
less and incurable ; from the first page to the last
the book is strewn with the mental wreckage of a
man who has prided himself on his own insanity,
and discovered, when it was too late to repair the
mishap, that he is still in his right mind, and has
no reasonable hope of getting out of it. Possibly it
he had begun the work earlier in life he might have
successfully escaped from his own senses, but
Tolstoi is now too old for such experiments, ihe
moral of the “ Kreutzer Sonata ” is that a man
whose sole occupations in life are to loaf and to
wallow in sensuality is apt to become tired and
disgusted, and this much the world knew before.
What the world did not know is that the old,
weary, burst-up voluptuary had any cause to write
a book for the express purpose of demanding why
he is tired and miserable and useless, but iolstoi
has written the book, and apparently regards the
contents thereof as a new_ revelation. It is a
volume of dreary introspection perpetrated by a
man who seems to suffer under an impression that
his own particular boil and his private tumour are
subjects in which the whole earth must necessarily
feel interested, though in a few days the “Kreutzer
Sonata ’’ will fall into oblivion, because the earth
has its own boil and its own tumour to look after.
The hero of the work is a p i rson who sits by him-
self in a mental desert contemplating bis symp-
toms, and hunting up fresh ones, and a person of
this kind is only of passing interest. He is the
sort of individual who would write an ode
upon his diseased liver, or would retire
into a wilderness with nothing but a bad
case of leprosy to keep him company, and
actually enjoy the companionship. Under the
influence of different surroundings he would
be a plain, morbid individual with a porous-
plaster, and a respirator, and a vast collection of
patent medicines, and he would send a guinea to
L. L. Smith on the ground that he didn’t feel
well; but accident has made him take refuge in
philosophy instead of pills, just as another acci-
dent might have led him to abandon both philo-
sophy and pills and take to gin. He has two
theories, neither of them being of any particular
service; one is that the noblest, if not the only,
mission of woman is to perpetuate the human
race, while the other is that the human race
should not be perpetuated, but should be allowed
to die out. The first is the inspiration of an
animal who mistakes his animalism for soul, and
his dull sensuality for poetic fervour, and who
fancies that his moral disease is the beginning of
a new gospel. When that ordinary clod, George
11., issued his ultimatum that there was no good
in “ baintingand boedry,” because his own sodden
brain could not rise above adultery and beer, he did
not extinguish these arts, for the world took no
notice of him, and yet, like Tolstoi’s hero, he pro-
bably regarded his brutal dullness as the sign
of an elevated mind and a great intellect. And
as for the second proposition, it is only a large,
vague, second-hand speculation at the best, and
twenty million people have formulated the same
idea before. If it would be good for mankind not
to exist, then it would be better for it never to
have existed, and, best of all, if time and space
and the principle of vitality, and the Influence
which called these things into being, had never
been at all. Man often dreams, in a hazy, uncer-
tain way, that he would like to bring about this
general abolition of everything, and then, realising
that the contract is too large for him, returns
to earthly considerations, and gets drunk to
drown the sense of his own impotence. It is a
miserable end to the vision of Nirvana, and next
day the dreamer is fined 7s. 6d. at the instance of
a common, earthy material policeman, who con-
fuses the visionary yearning after annihilation with
the purely human tendency to tear his uniform.
Tolstoi’s hero confesses frankly that he was
muddled with liquor and tobacco during the
greater part of his career. He commenced life as
a rake, and used up his capacity for living when
still in his boyhood. Then he married, and was
surprised to find himself too worn and battered
and prematurely old to extract' any fresh enjoy-
ment out of his leprous existence. Finally he
became a sodden toper, with maudlin yearnings
after the sublime, the infinite, and the unfixable in
between his beers, and in between his yearnings
he knocked his wife about and raised promiscuous
Gehenna in his palace, till at last he ended by
jumping on the unhappy lady with both feet and
performing a matter-of-fact murder of the Bill
Sikes pattern. Tolstoi practically maintains
that the world wants a new Gospel to keep this
common animal —for even his yearnings are
common, and his philosophy is borrowed, and
there is nothing new about him—from going wrong,
but unluckily such an animal isimperviousto gospel
of any kind. He is not obscene or offensive, though
he possibly aimed at being both. Neither is he
interesting ; he is merely dull and egotistical, and
he demands that the world shall be abolished
because he has been a drunkard and a failure, and
his liver has gone awry. He is only a new edition
of that flaring Sunday-school oleograph in which
a horizontal expiring family point a moral in a
garret, accompanied by a gin-bottle, while a
strictly vertical dissenting parson stands alongside
and reads an appropriate chapter from the writings
of Solomon. The “ Kreutzer Sonata ”is a deadly
stupid publication with a few medical speculations
slung in here and there, and when the medical
speculations grow stale it will fall into hopeless
oblivion. The story is one which can be read
every day in the columns of the Evening News,
and when condensed by a cheap, unimpressionable
reporter it reads something like this :
John Smith, a brutal-looking individual of no occupa-
tion, was charged with a murderous assault upon hia
wife. The plaintiff, who was in a terribly-battered con-
dition, testified that he was perpetually intoxicated, and
had quarrelled with her and ill-used her almost from the
day of their marriage. Sentenced to three months’ im-
prisonment with hard labour.
If some unwholesome Tolstoi would seize upon
this paragraph, and depict John Smith as a person
with wild, unfathomable longings after an ideal
state of society, he would have all the necessary
materials for a new “ Kreutzer Sonata.”—Yours,
&c.,
TITUS SALT.
Some coarse person has upset the Sabbath
calm of Hobart by an outrage which has no
parallel in history since the burning of Rome. He
began bis nefarious proceedings by raiding the
premises of a local chemist, and taking a glass
dish full of human teeth, which that gentleman
had jerked out of various patients in the course of
a long and honourable career. There was the
molar that had been dynamited out of the coun-
tenance of a fainting maiden dressed in white,
and the one which had been extracted gratis from
a poor widow, who was unable to pay, and whom
the chemist had dragged six times round the room
by the tooth in obedience to that scriptural in-
junction which bids us comfort the bereaved and
give a leg up to the fatherless. Also, there was
the grinder that had been wrested from the
starving and staggered head of a true Christian
who made the room vociferous with his profanity
while the proceedings were going on, and never
paid the bill afterwards; and the other one
that the operator removed out of the rose-
bud mouth of his own best girl when they
were both young, and life was a primrose path of
loveliness. He took this particular patient on his
knee, and rested her head on his shoulder during
the operation, and then she went and married
another fellow, and when ho looked back on the
episode he often wished he had simply grasped her
tooth in a cold, mercenary fashion, as if it was the
tooth of an utter stranger, and hauled her all over
the house like an ordinary customer, till at last
the root gave way and let her down in a heap on
the floor. There were a lot of pleasant associations
about that accumulation of teeth, and when the
thief sloped with them he left a void behind him
which will never again be filled. Then having
secured his prey, that debased individual went
into the shop of the adjacent butcher, and hove
the teeth all over the meat, after which he
smiled a smooth and treacherous smile
and departed. The butcher, unhappily, was
an absent-minded man, and he sent the meat and
ths teeth together to his customers, and later in
the day people with teeth in their pockets began
to drop in by twos and threes and offer remarks
which nearly blew the head off the miserable
artisan in flesh, and they also made baseless asser-
tions all over the shop, and offered unsupported
statements in the back-yard. Most of their obser-
vations were unfit to appear in print and must,
therefore, be suppressed, but anyone who can
imagine the language of a whole oityful of people
with loose teeth in their hash can readily fill in
the deficiency. It was an exciting time, and
Hobart never wants another such upheaval.
FREETRDE OR PROTECTION.
Some vote Protection, some Freetrade,
As best for this young country’s weal.
But ask the youth or blushing maid,
And each will answer — Pemell’s Meal I
As best, because it has been tried,
There’s nothing half so good beside.
His Golden Meal is what I mean,
’Tis good and pure and cheap, I wean.

The present session in the Queensland
Assembly has been a session of conundrums.
Glassey asks the latest as follows: “By what
constitutional law was the ex-reverend editor
Brentnall elected to the Legislative Council?”
The answer isn’t very far to seek. Parson Brent-
nall is in the Building Society syndicating swim,
who pull the political strings just how they like in
Brisbane.
Premier Parkes indignantly declares that
“if men outside Parliament could say what mem-
bers were to have in the way of refreshments, they
might just as well decide what kind oi seats they
were to sit upon.” As a matter of fact, men out-
side the House do decide upon this seat subject.
And quite a number of the present members will
soon find it decided that their seats arete be placed
outside the House for the future.
Properly does the southern press point out
that it is impossible for Munro to give due atten-
tion to the duties of Premier. He is up to the
eyes in business, with his Federal Bank, Federal
Building Society, immense interests at King
Sound, West Australia, and the two big Coffee
Palaces, the Grand and Victoria. He imagines
that the fag-end of his mind will do for a Premier-
ship, though such was never the idea of profes-
sional politicians like Berry and Gillies.

Although the Northern grandees may not
acknowledge the fact now, an eccentric old fruit-
merchant named Marshall is really the father of
the N. Q. Separation movement. For a while,
the persevering enthusiast was simply regarded as
a cranky old faddist, but in time the Townsville
land syndicators—and their name is legion—
caught on, and then the separatist propaganda
spread like a cholera epidemic right through the
North. The present writer well remembers the
first separation meeting held at Townsville, and
the contumely which the local press heaped upon
its conveners. But all that is passed now. After
Marshall had borne the heat and burden of the
day, numbers were ready to step in and take the
credit.
Melbourne Savings Bank Committee have
issued circulars with a panacea for Strikes, which
is—to scrape up acquaintance with them. We used
to be one of those, not bank directors, but theft-
panaceans, but see clearly that it doesn’t go all
round. “Ah, I’ve putt somethink by,’’ was the
smug saying of a few strikists —who were
struck aghast when it was suggested (perchance
by some six-pound-a-week man) that they ought
to eat up the putt by. Wherefore the putt by man
slunk from his fellows, and had no camaraderie.
After all, the man who hid his talent in a napkin
was the champion putt by. You can putt by as
much as you like if you shirk all the responsi-
bilities of life.
Premier Playford, of South Australia,
offers the following refreshing observations: “I
do not object to any man taking a title if he has
worked hard and done good service for the
country, and likes to take it, but the taking of a title
THE MARCH OF SCIENCE.
Some one having invented a smokeless, noiseless and odourless powder, it is now in
order for somebody to go one better and invent a smokeless and odourless tobacco for use
in tramways, churches, etc.
to hand down to his children and his chi'dren’s
children, who may be idiots, or drunkards, or
fools, or cowards, is something those colonies
ought not to countenance. A man’s descendants
may possibly be deserving of a title, but then they
should have it on their own merit.” This is good
enough so far as it goes, but what Playford really
wants to convey is that every man should get his
title by his own meanness, instead of relying on
the meanness of his ancestors. Nobody ever gets
a title by merit nowadays, or because he has done
good to anybody. Who ever conceived the idea of
making Dr. Livingstone a duke, for example? or
of giving a knighthood to the. unobtrusive doctor
who found a specific for snakebite?
Let Parnell stick to his leadership while
bis party trust him politically ; when they
abandon him let him resign. Berry was the
Robespierre of the Victorian revolution ; and this
is what the Age, which now condemns Parnell as
infamous, said about Berry, a year or two before
the latter became its prophet :
It has been very properly left to Mr. Garrett to make
his statement in unmistakeable terms ! He has done so,
and Mr. Berry complains that the charge of infamy has
been shifted from his father-in-law to himself. His quasi
denial is therefore not inconsistent with the interpreta-
tion put upon his resignation as a confession of down-
right infamy on his own part. This is the only feasible
interpretation that can he put upon his movement of last
night, and it is well he should know it. It is not only a
confession of guilt of some sort, hut the silence of his
colleagues is an indication that they consider him
o-uilty. . . . H unfit to be an honorable member of
the Executive Council, he is unfit to he an honorable
member of the Legislative Assembly. Even a final
acquittance by the select committee would not, after
what has transpired, rehabilitate his character. Setting
aside altogether Mr. Berry’s private affairs, we have no
hesitation in denouncing his appointment as politically
infamous.
The Quarterly Review, formerly edited
by Lockhart, Sir Walter Scott’s son-in-law, has
been favoured with an advance copy of Sir
Walter’s diary, from 1825 to 1832, nothing what-
ever from which has hitherto been published.
Scott was prompted to start a diary through
Murray, the publisher, showing him Byron’s.
The book will be out in a mail or two.

virtuous peer-apparent, unsullied by the lightest
breath of scandal and beloved by all who knew
him. But then he was only three years of age
when England mourned his loss.

Ibsen, the Norwegian dramatist, makes
his wife copy out all his writings, and employs
her as secretary, factotum, and clerical drudge
generally. In theory he holds that a wife should
lead her own existence, but in pract ico he drives
an exhausted little woman in literary harness
without asking her opinion on the subject. But,
perhaps she likes it.

appear certain portraits of D. W. B. Sladen, in
various garbs and attitudes, which give us further
proof of the ad vertising capabilities of this chame-
leonic individual. Sladen poses as a Britisher in
Australia, as an Australian in Canada, as a Cana-
dian in Japan; possibly be will eventually turn
up as a Japanese in England.

Conversation between Judge Chubb and
an aboriginal witness at Charters Towers (Q.) :
His Honor : “ You are a Christian?” Witness :
‘ Yes.” His Honor : “ You know God ?” Wit-
ness : “Yes.” His Honor: “Where is God?”
Witness: “You all same God!” Had this
occurred to Mr. Justice Pope A. Cooper he would
have regarded it as merely a neatly-turned and
well-deserved compliment.
Henry Kendall’s verses on Ninian Mel-
ville are by far the roughest lines ever written by
a well-recognised verse-writer in Australia. They
occur in a very few copies of “ Songs from the
Mountains.” The eye of the publisher caught
them on the morning of issue, and the edition was
suppressed until the obnoxious verses had been
struck out. “Ninny of the nimble tongue ” would
give something to prove the sale of a copi r .
We read that the Earl of Devon, son of a
vice-president of the English Church Union, and
brother-in-law of Viscount Halifax, president of
that body ; Sir Philip Rose, Bart., Lady Rose and
family, Lady Ellis, wife of an ex Lord Mayor;
Rev. T. P. Duthie, late chaplain to the Earl of
Kintore; Kegan Paul, head of the great publish-
ing firm; Francillon, managing editor of the
Globe ; Harrington, of tubular bell fame; and
Colonel Houghton, of Crimea and India fighting
renown, have all just ’verted to R.C.-isin.
Raking up Munro’s never - forgotten
“Never, never, never,” puts us in mind of the
“I shot a man wanst ” of the former Victorian
M.P., known as Chief Baron O’Hea, who had the
ill-luck once to travel in the train to Colao with
two strangers, who were running-down the Berry
Government, when the Baron put in his spoke
with “Jintlemin, I shot a man wanst for saying
less than you’re saying now.” This stuck to him
as long as he lived. The satirical prints were ever
chucldng up “I shot a man wanst,” so that he
could have bitten off his tongue for saying it.
Lord Rosebery was entertaining a large
party of civic magnates in the country. One of
them, whose acquaintance with the ways of
fashionable life was of the slightest, making his
first experiment with ice pudding, took an exten-
sive mouthful, and suffered agonies of chill as the
frozen mass slowly dissolved. Then he repaired
to the host and intimated that there had been
some mistake in the kitchen—the pudding had got
frozen. Rosebery listened without moving a
muscle, tasted the stuff, thanked his informant,
and said he would speak to the cook. Then he
whispered to one of the attendants, came back
with a relieved face, and told the honest farmer,
“ It’s all right. They tell me this is a new sort of
pudding that is frozen on purpose.” There is
probably, remarks the paper which tells the story,
no more perfect instance of good manners on
record. N.B. —This is a chestnut; those re-
frigerated stories keep quite fresh for a very long
time.

It has now, we trust, become evident
from sworn testimony in the courts that the
noble Australian who hires a private detective
to watch his wicked wife, is simply pi’oviding that
wife with another paramour, and paying that
paramour for his attentions. It reminds us of the
man who hired a cab to drive a ballet-girl home to
supper at her lodgings. Said the cabman to him,
after getting his big midnight fare : “Clear out!”
“ What do you mean?” asked the gay one. “I
mean,” said the burly cabman, “that you have
paid me for driving my own wife home. Clpar
out!”
* * *
It will be contended on behalf of plaintiff
in a forthcoming N.S.W. slander action that no
privilege legally attends slanderous statements
made in Parliament. Arguments which primd
facie are very strong will be adduced in support
of this startling contention.
* # * *
If the crowd of Australian amateur
evangelists who went off by the MeUmuir to con-
vert the Chinese Empire are a fair sample, the
persons engaged in this Sisyphean task must be a
poor lot. A less intelligent or more commonplace
looking lot of people you never saw gathered
together in a photograph.
* * *
It has just transpired that if the police are
engaged in arresting a drunken woman who kicks
and call upon you to assist them in their task, you
are bound to comply, under the penalty of being
committed for trial. How much would the aver-
age man catch hold of a drunken woman’s feet
for? (The police would be sure to offer you that
end of her.) Speaking for ourselves, we would
rather tell the police, as did those young Maitland
men, to do their dirty work themselves, and
take our chance of being sent to gaol.
* # #
The facts of the dispute—founded on an
anonymous note — between the S.M. Herald and
the Associated Shipowners would make a chapter
of merry reading. That any sane business man
should in the 19th century worry about an abusive
nameless letter instead of flipping it into the dust-
bin almost surpasses belief.
o # #
Stipendiary Magistrate Lee, of Sydney,
says that “ the police have a perfect right at any
time to enter any dwelling if they have reasonable
grounds for believing that a felony is being com-
mitted or that a felon of whom they are in search
is concealed within.” Indeed ! And, pray, what
is going to stop the honest house-father from
putting four bullets into the chest of the clumsy
policeman who gets through the window, under
the influence of reasonable but ill-founded suspi-
cions, at 2 a.m?
* * *
Ex-Governor Carington has written from
the mail-steamer a most humble letter (designed,
of course, for publication) to the effect that the
more he thinks over the matter, the more he feels
that he was utterly unworthy of the “send-off’
given him. We may inform the Baron that that
is exactly the conclusion arrived at by the com-
munity which gave the “send-off” aforesaid.
* # #
It was mentioned at a recent meeting of
the Australian Women’s Christian Temperance
Union that the fermented wine used for sacra-
mental purposes was “ abominable and accursed —
the devil in solution, in fact.” A Mrs. Fullwood
told a story of how, by some means, the unfer-
mented wine was recently forgotten at a suburban
church until too late. “ The minister in his diffi-
culty appealed to one of his members, a white
ribbonite and said, ‘ I suppose I*must not use port
wine?’ ‘Certainly not,’she replied. They tried
to make a drop of raisin wine, but were unsuccess-
ful, and as a last resort a gentleman got a tin of
raspberry-jam, poured some boiling water over it,
strained it, and produced an excellent wine, which
was used in the sacrament that night.” You can
buy raspberry jam for fourpenee a tin and hot
water is cheap enough, but what a revoltingly
prosaic religion must that be the imagination of
whose followers—generally fanatics, by the same
token —does not soar above ration-sugar and
turnip-seed ! A creed in which “ a lady ” settles
matters by saying “certainly not ”to the high-
priest, and in which “a gentleman” strains
raspberry jam in order to typify something which
is venerated by millions of Christians and adored
by millions more, is a blasphemy against the
human bump of veneration. No one is so obscene
as your frenzied religionist.
When J. Wyllie, alias the “Herd Laddie,”
the champion draughts-player, went to Queens-
land some weeks ago, the Brisbane Draughts Club
arranged to lodge him at one of the leading hotels,
where the tariff was £3 10s. per week. After he
had been there a week, the canny Scot acciden-
tally discovered the amount which was being paid
on his account to the hotel-keeper, and intimated
to the club secretary that he would rather (d’ye
ken) receive the money and pay for his own board.
Naturally the club humoured the old man and
complied with his request, with the result that,
on the following week, the Herd Laddie roosted
in a modest hash-house on Spring Hill at the rate
of £1 a week, with washing. Fact.
* » #
The “ Herd Laddie” is a very “dry-hash”
individual; the only champion that has ever
come under our notice who doesn’t smoke,
drink, swear and lie about his own prowess. He
is also a simple-minded, though acquisitive soul,
who cares little what lie eats so long as he is fed,
who usually tells the waiter, when travelling, to
bring him “ onything,” and who gravitates in his
bibulous tastes between ginger-beer and tea.
Wylie must have a very long stocking somewhere.
He charges a shilling a game for the pleasure
of playing with him, and should his an-
tagonist fail at once to dump down the
coin, says suddenly, in a hard and dispassionate
voice that jerks the needful out of the other
fellow’s pocket; “ Whaur’s yer shulllin’;” Then
he seems to close his eyes and go off in a doze till
his adversary “moves,” when with one swoop he
picks off about six of the best “men,” and again
dozes off like an alligator waiting for its prey,
while gently rattling the captured checkers with
his left hand. They invited the old man once to
play in a N.S.W. working men’s club, and paid
him £3 for the night, but when his first anta-
gonist (a burly miner) presented himself, his
battle-cry of “Whaur’s yer shullin'?” boomed
forth as usual. That night Wylie played and won
about 60 games at “a bob a nob,” and thus, by
the_ exercise of a little canniness, doubled his
takings. Wylie, Whaur’s yer shullins?
SPEECHES FROM THE AUSTRALIAN DOCK—No. 6.
STIPENDIARY (to gentleman behind the spikes on a charge of righteous conduct): “It won’t
do for you to plead that you did not know you were violating the law.
Ignorance of the law is no excuse for any man.”
PRISONER : “That’s rather rough on me an’ you, ain’t it, yer Washup?”
The latest Melbourne seduction case, in
which £350 damages were awarded to the girl’s
father, was, like about ten which have preceded
it, the outcome of after-church walks.
* * #
Asa step towards abolishing wigs and
gowns one may fairly object to that awful appa-
ratus of a scarlet Mother Shipton Richelieu robe
worn by the criminal judge, with his full-bottomed
wig, its long flappers specially designed to pre-
vent him from hearing the evidence by muffling
up his ears, so that Justice is deaf as well as
blind. On the dog-days the judge is supposed to
sit with a full suit of black under his Mother
Shipton, and then he has to get a twelvemonth
holiday for a trip to Europe, because he has not
been properly ventilated.
# # *
It is interesting to note that one of the old
residences of “Margaret Oatchpole’’stood at the
corner of Liverpool and Elizabeth streets, Sydney,
and was purchased by Dr. Renwick who pulled
down the old house and erected on the ground the
fine terrace next his private residence.
# * *
Quong Tart, the enterprising Sydney
Chinese merchant, entertained a party of
aborigines the other day at the N.S.W. Zoological
Gardens. The last time the present writer heard
of a Chinese gentleman entertaining the aborigines
was in the Far North. P.S.—The darkies picked
his bones.
# * *
An 18-year-old larrikin to whom Judge
Wilkinson gave the tremendous sentence of seven
years the other day at Sydney Quarter Sessions,
expressed the hope that the Judge would be
“ struck blind ” as soon as possible. The
Bulletin is of opinion that the law which con-
demns a mere boy to consort with cast-iron
criminals in a convict prison until he attains the
age of irreclaimability must be stone-blind already.
# * *
A tramway existed in Pitt-street, Sydney,
a generation ago. It had to be. taken up in conse-
quence of loss of life and congestion of traffic, and
yet the present Government want to re-establish
the system.
• * *
Since removing from the old police head-
quarters to the new edifice in Melbourne, a novel
system of “passing the doctor” has been in-
augurated for those applicants required to undergo
medical examination as a preliminary. After
overhauling the candidate the doctor requests him
to pass on through a door which lie specifies with
a wave of his hand. Presuming that he is to wait
for further investigations or formalities in an
adjoining apartment the patient aspirant is much
astonished on emerging to find himself in the
street, when the hideous truth in all its nakedness
dawns upon him, and with muttered execrations
he wends sadly towards the nearest public-house.
* * *
A Richmond (Melb.) man, who lives in a
£7500 house, has been ca.sa.’d for an £lB 10s.
tailor’s bill. He pleaded that he was unable
to pay a penny, being employmentless,
and that the property was his wife’s. Not
long ago, in the same locality, a man
was sued for the value of paint supplied, and
which had been applied to his wife’s house. She
pleaded that she had never authorised the pur-
chase of the paint, and that it had been procured
by her husband in his own name and entered to
his account by the tradesman while she was away.
Verdict for defendant. “ Two souls with but
a single thought ” and two separate banking
accounts.
* * *
The proposed Bill for restraining barristers’
tongues might go a little further and deal gently
with his Honor’s summing-up. Delivering judg-
ment in the Watson v. Cain case, Mr. Justice
Hood said of Miss Watson that “shehad engaged
herself to Mr. Kitz after an acquaintance of about
15 or 16 days, and was living in his house away
from her mother.” The young lady’s solicitors
explain that Mr. Kitz has no house of his own,
but lives with his brother and sister-in-law, at
whose place Miss Watson was staying as their
guest. A newspaper originating the mis-state-
ment which the judge made with impunity would
have been promptly “ pulled ” for damages, and
heavy damages, too.
# # *
His Excellency Sir William F, C. Robin-
son, the first full-grown Governor of Western
Australia, tried to get into a Melbourne tramcar
the other day while it was in motion, but missed
his footing and made an undignified exhibition of
himself in the public thoroughfare. If he had
been left to himself he would simply have turned
a somersault and fallen with a hideous “ squash ”
in the mud, but the conductor dragged him in
backwards by the hair, with his hat crushed down
over his ears, and his umbrella broken in seven
places. It was a miserable show, but a viceroy
going head first into a moving vehicle and a king
when he is drunk and standing on all fours on a
doorstep, are both weighed down by necessity,
and the laws of gravitation are too strong for
them.
* * *
A clergyman avHo recently visited Pialba,
the fashionable but priestless seaside resort of
Maryborough (Q.), was asked to officiate in the
church on the Sunday. The soul-saver replied
that he was down there for a holiday, but that, if
they really wanted him, his terms were two
guineas per sermon.
* * *
The Queensland parliamentary refresh-
ment-room has been a barred Eden to Brisbane
reporters ever since a wicked old man (who is also
an M.P.) took a scissors and partially deprived
one sleeping literary Samson of his fiery looks.
Now, any member who desires to treat “the only
reporter who ever does me justice ” has perforce
to carry the drink up to the gallery, or seduce the
reporter down to the neighbouring pub.
* * *
They say that a leading Adelaide Society
lady means to have revenge for the ’aughty con-
duct of the —let us say-governess of one of the
vice-regal residences whereby the lady aforesaid
suffered the indignity of a severe snub on a recent
brilliant occasion.
“ Hullo, pappy !” said a Sydney pressman
to another recently. “ I know what you’re hinting
at,” said the virtuous and indignant individual
thus addressed, “but it’s fhe child of the Cabinet
and they’ve simply laid a vile plot to discredit one
of their bitterest and most dangerous antagonists
It’s an Australian parallel of tb° Barnell-O’Shea
case. ”
* * *
One of the largest and most richly-gilded
house-owners and usurers in Sydney and suburbs
prefers to rent his tenements to the demi-monde
who pay fatter rents than more honest people!
The police ought to raid a dozen of his tenements
on one day, and have it publicly stated in Court
who is the owner.
» * *
Mrs. Harrison Lee stated at the Women’s
Christian Temperance Union, in Melbourne, that
when recently in a country court she had seen a
drunken woman sentenced to 12 months’ im-
prisonment by the publican who had served her
with liquor ! That could not be in N.S.W., as no
publican is there allowed to be a magistrate. But
we have seen a N.S.W. J.P. issue a prohibition-
order against a tippler, and we have seen the same
J.P. half-an-hour afterwards take sixpence from
the tippler, and, entering a public-house, buy a
drink and hand it to the aforesaid tippler outside
and that was in the town of Cowra, bedad !
* * »
What a place Melbourne is for sleep-walk-
ing women ! A widow was arrested in Fitzroy the
other evening at the midnight hour, parading the
tram-track arrayed only in her robe de nuit.
# # #
The drowning of Mrs. Maud Fleming,
aged 28, and her daughter Olga aged 10 at Rose
Bay, Sydney, through theupsettingof a dingy, when
the lady’s young son was saved by the coachman,
who was rowing the boat, and who swam ashore
with the boy 150 yards, is one of those affairs
which, for the ten-thousandth time emphasises the
fact that no woman should ever be permitted to
go out in a boat so frail that it may be capsized bv
one or two persons leaning over the side.
* * #
Should Kintore venture his trip across
Australia, he will be accompanied by Bews, the
S.A. Minister of Education. “The Gov.” is
going to purchase a camel to train on at Adelaide
Government House.
* # #
A vice-regal elopement is reported. One
of Governor Kintore’s gardeners has eloped with
a young woman attached to the household retinue.
Adelaide Government House went without dinner
on the festive occasion, but as Kmty was in
Melbourne it didn’t much matter.
* * *
There will be considerable opposition to
Kintore’s projected tour from Port Darwin to
Adelaide, especially if the long-suffering taxpayer
has to empty his pockets again. What earthly
South Australia will derive from such a
trip it is difficult to imagine, unless it will be the
amusement to be obtained by reading of the
escapades of the amateur explorers. “Kinty”
will probably throw it over when the supreme
moment comes if he has to find the necessary
“bawbees.”
# * *
It is said that one of the conditions of the
creation of anew baronetcy now is that the Bart,
to be honoured shall settle £IOO,OOO on his heir
before receiving the title.
* * *
If it be really true that a Sydney Sewerage
Board official has been sacrificed as a peace-
offering to the “ dignity ” of the Mayor of Sydney,
under the circumstances set forth in the press by
the dismissed official, no more contemptible civic
scandal has transpired for many a day. Mayor
Burdekin owes it to his own self-respect as a man
to fully acquaint the public with the facts so far
as he is cognisant of them.
# * *
Rev. S. S. Moncrieff, an Anglican clergy-
man at Moonta (S.A.), has been fined £2 and costs
for thrashing a small boy. The patfson smilingly
offered to “ take it out,” but the Bench objected.
# * *
The story goes that a certain big, leading
southern daily is shortly to find its way into a
“limited ” company.
# * *
Prince Albert Victor planted a pine tree
in Adelaide Botanical Gardens in 1881, in
“ honour ”of his visit. The pine has had a
miserable existence, is ill-grown, and voted by
visitors to be about the ugliest tree in the place.
Consequently, &c., &c.
# * #
The Melbourne Sabbatarians are again
rampant. At the Post-office people are allowed
to come on Sundays and take their own letters out
of the private boxes. Now, this, you know, is an
insidious step towards opening the Public Library.
Who knows what may be in the letters?
Literature, maybe. Then it takes people’s minds
off the kirk, thinking of their letters. A letter
may do as much mischief as the ballet.

Now that the time approaches for the
withdrawal of all Kanaka labour, several big
Oueenslandsugar-planters andseizetheopportunity
file their schedules. Asa matter of fact, the

# * *
jt is stated that at Cooktown the other
day the two sons of a dying woman boarded a
newly-arrived A.U.S.N. Co.’s steamer to beg for
iittle ice to soothe their mother’s sufferings—
none being procurable in the town. The boon was
summarily denied, the official appealed to growl-
ing out that somebody was always dying when he
visited Cooktown. A more influential appeal ob-
tained the ice next morning, but by the time it
reached the doctor’s hands the patient was dead.
* * *
From the periodical glimpses which the
Australian public get of the social life of Norfolk
Island, it is quite evident that the island is a place
where people have nothing to do but sing psalms,
make hot love, pray, slander their neighbours, and
drink rum whenever it is to be got.
* * *
An honest young Welshman named Ellis
Evans, probably out of work, has just got a
month’s gaol for hooting Governor Kintore outside
Adelaide Theatre Royal. Had Evans cheered
that freckled magnate all would have been well.
Hypocrisy is the only thing that pays a dividend
in this world.
* * *
A “representative” Australian took up
his quarters at a fashionable London hotel, after
careful enquiry made to prevent any loss of
dignitv through going to the wrong house. In
the hall-porter’s book his name stood with the
letters M.L.A.,” after it, and with a
feeling of awe the guardian watched him ascend
the stairs, for never previously had he seen such a
mysterious tail to a name. Following closely
upon the Australian’s heels came another guest,
a gentleman from Lancashire, the master of the
band which had just won the championship
prize in the “ Counties ” contest, and had now
been brought to town, “by request,” to play
before Her Gracious at Windsor. Asked to sign
his name also in the book, he at first demurred, as
not knowing the customs of the people amongst
whom he stayed; then he asked what all those
letters meant, but the porter really could not say,
the gentleman’s title, most likely, as they “mostly
alius ” put them in the book, but he didn’t rightly
know what “them” stood for. “And am 1 to
put my title down too ?” “ Certainly, if the
gentleman pleased,” and he signed his name with
the addition of the letters 8.8.8.8.8.8.8. r lhe
porter took one glance and retired, merely mutter-
ing, “ Well, I’m and !” The visitor remarked
that he thought, that let “the other out,” and
if anybody wanted to know any more about him,
why he was the Best Blanked Bugle Blower in
the Bolton Brass Band. But the clerk had made
some mistake about the rooms, and they were
“ really sorry ” that they were unable to accom-
modate the gentleman.

It was queer to sit in the Melbourne
Equity Court, and listen to Judge Molesworth’s
own will being put through, with the remem-
brance of the 15,000 wills which the Judge had
put through in his time. Mr. Phnnky use to rise
with “ May it please your Honor—In the matter
of the will of then old Moley would sniff his
“What? Hey?” and the name would nearly
always go down first, with the scratching quill,
as Smithers for Blithers, “ Withers ?” “ Blithers”
“ Smithers ?”
“Some of our hanging judges, writes
“Maroogaline,” “may advantageously spend a
few minutes in solemn reflection over the follow-
ing well-authenticated fact from the history of
Queensland On 26th April, 1848, Robert Cox, a
timber-getter from the Tweed River, was mur-
dered at Kangaroo Point, Brisbane. For this
murder a man named William Fife was tried and
hanged in Sydney, asserting his innocence to the
last. Then, years afterwards, a man confessed on
his death-bed, not only that he was the actual
murderer of Cox, but that he stood among the
crowd of spectators at the gallows and saw the
innocent Fife hanged ! I have recently read the
whole of the evidence taken at the trial, and it
left in me a mournful belief that the early judges
only required a fairly plausible excuse to hang
anybody. The benefit of any doubt that existed
was cheerfully given to the gallows.”
Phonetic Frauds.
The roll of martial drum, an anthem’s swell,
The shout of mirth, the shriek from prison cell,
The grating whirr of wheels, the ocean’s moaning,
A wedding peal, the solemn knell’s intoning,
The cannon’s roar, and peaceful vesper bell,
The thunder’s crash, the murmur of the shell,
A trumpet’s brazen blast, the hum of bees,
A symphony of zephyrs ’mong the trees ;
The dingo’s howl, the luring bell-bird’s note
(Becussed by bushies whom we may not quote,
What time the tinkling tune of bells remote
Seems far and near through sunrise air to float);
The Sheoaks’ holy whisper, the Cocky’s squall,
A bullock-puncher’s “ prayer,” the lulling water-
fall— _
Are but vibrations of the atmosphere—
Acoustic tricks upon the human ear.
The warhlings of the lark when skyward winging,
His gushing rhapsodies at Heaven’s gate singing
May be but feathered fits, for aught we know,
Or soaring swear-words at his mate below. _
And who can gauge the glee or sound the sigh
Concentred in the Curlew’s wild, weird cry ?

The Wide Bay and Burnett News_(Q.)
is the latest journal to declare emphatically
against capital punishment.
The Commissioner of Lunacy, says a S. F.
contemp., had to pass upon a very sad case the
other day. It was that of a stock-broker, who
conceived a singular infatuation for his own wife.
A Parisian landlord is doing his best to
combat the alleged depopulation of France. He
will only accept as tenants married couples with
children. Every time a birth takes place in one of
his houses, he gives the mother, ten francs, a
chicken and her winter coal. He is the father of
seventeen healthy children.

(Suppose, if you please, a Lord High Admiral in Her Majesty’s Service to have died of ennui
and over-feeding. He applies at the straight and narrow gate for admittance.)

below that of a Salvation Army corporal.”

Brisbane wharf-labourers who have got
back to work are donating 25 per cent, of their
earnings to their less fortunate brethren.
The S.A. wheat harvest is estimated to
yield an average of nine bushels per acre. It is
calculated that the farmers will divide between
them about £2,500,000 as the result.

At a Parisian restaurant lately an elegantly-
attired man dropped in, ate his way right through
an expensive bill of fare and drank a bottle of wine,
which ran his account up to about £3.- Then he
called for the bill, and when it was brought he
solemnly put it on the table in front of him, and
blew out his brains. Consequently the restaurateur
is £3 out, and the brains flew all over a regular
customer sitting at the next table, and irritated
him to such an extent that he now dines else-
where, which is a serious addition to the loss.
Dymock, the Sydney bookseller, tells the
following yarn -He once had a very rare book
which he exhibited one morning in the front of his
shop, in the hope that it would catch the eye of
some antiquarian. In the afternoon a gentlemanly
stranger came to Dymock with a copy of the same
book for sale. Dymock was surprised at seeing
another copy of the same work, as his was the
only one he had ever possessed. After some
haggling the bookseller and the stranger came to
terms, the latter received his cash and departed,
and the former took the book to the front to com-
pare with the other copy. Tableau ! It was gone !
Dymock had paid a rattling good price for one of
Ids own books.
From Manly Beach to Rock Lily is about
nine miles. Yet between these two points in a
district which has been settled for perhaps
30 years, you hardly see a home with a garden
attached to it. About four years ago L6on
Houreux, an industrious Frenchman, bought five
acres of swampy ground at Rock Lily, which ho
proceeded to drain and plant. Sydney people
should now visit his place that they may see of what
such ground is capable, and learn for the first time
how mere ignorance and laziness hang like a pall
upon a country which should smile with fruit and
flowers. M. Houreux, who keeps a neat little inn
where excellent meals may be had, now runs a
cheap coach-line between Manly and his hostelry.
The Austrian Government has been ex-
perimenting with the new smokeless powder in
some military manoeuvres in Hungary, and feels
bad over the result. One army was hidden among
some woodson the side of a hill, while the other
was supposed to be attacking the position, and
the stationary force pounded away for four hours
without once giving the enemy a chance to dis-
cover its position. The pale-grey uniforms were
completely lost among the trees and scrub; the
flashes were so slight that they were invisible at
ordinary range; and as for the reports they were
mixed and multiplied by the echoes till they
seemed to be everywhere, and nowhere in parti-
cular. The British soldier in his glaring, blood-
red coat will have a cheerful and refreshing time
in the next campaign. He is the most conspicuous
fighting man in the world, and unless he takes off
his clothes and goes into battle with nothing on
but his gun and his boots he is bound to be exter-
minated. But when two such armies as the
Austrians and Germans meet, the one which hides
itself most effectually will win, and if both hide
equally well then the one which sneezes first and
thereby betrays its situation must go under.
Sundry bones and other remains were dis-
covered lately on the Canterbury Plains (N.Z.),
and a local editor spreads himself on the subject
thus :
The spot where the discovery was made is lonely, deso-
late, and exceedingly dreary—just the place which seems
fitted to be the scene of a tragedy. The road winds round
the edge of a patch of dark woodland, and on the other
side is a stretch of swampy ground, while in neither
direction is a single habitation visible. Between the
fence and the swamp, hidden among the long grass and
scrub, the remains were found, &o.
There is a certain amount of descriptive power in
these lines, but still the place does not fill the bill,
and a dead body possessed of reasonable discrimina-
tion ought always to harmonise as much as possible
with its surroundings. Any common, ordinary
corpse might be satisfied to be found on the edge
of a swamp, but one that has an eye to dramatic
effect would be discovered in a cave on a murky
night, when the rain is falling with a sodden,
squashy sound among the dead leaves, and the pro-
ceedings should be accompanied, if possible, by
thumjer. Arrangements should be made for having
a blasted tree close to the spot with a raven in it.
Also the voice of a ghost should be heard in the
distance, and there should be a clanking of chains,
and a loud shriek, and a strange rushing sound in
the air, accompanied by groans. Furthermore,
the spectres of the deceased’s ancestors should
gibber among the tree trunks, unless otherwise
engaged, or detained by unforeseen circumstances,
or hindered by the weather, or incapacitated
through illness, or inability to catch the omnibus ;
and a"cold shiver—if one can be procnred—should
pervade the atmosphere. And these things having
been duly provided, wo would lay on a dull,
sickening thud of some kind—even if the thud is
an outside circumstance with no possible bearing
on the case, it doesn’t matter ; and we would have
a dismal morass in the background with a slimy
reptile attached, and then the tragic scene would
be about complete. The mangled victim who is
found under such conditions as these, and the
ordinary newspaper victim who is discovered in a
bald, uninteresting way with no surroundings
worth speaking of are two entirely distinct
victims ; and even a penny paper should be able
to appreciate the difference.
Dear Bulletin, —Your correspondent,
writing of “George’s” adventurous career, is,
although correct in his main facts, some-
times wrong in detail. Smith’s station was stuck-
up by two men, one of whom was afterwards shot
on the Etheridge. The incidents related of Smith
grappling at once with the robber, and George
clearing out, are quite true. The time Nation
(Nathan, he calls him) died was when George,
with Nation, Leonard and a black-boy, were
taking cattle over to Port Darwin. Leonard
got in to one of the telegraph-stations on the over-
land line and obtained rations and assistance to go
back for Nation, who was an old man, unfitted for
the hardship of the journey. He was dead when
the party reached him. George abandoned
the cattle and made the line higher up. Some
queer rumours were circulated at the time as to
the fiasco. Another mysterious disappearance of
a companion of George’s was on the Nicholson.
He and two men—one named Batten—were
camped on the river. Batten was in the
camp, George after the horses, and the
third man down at the water, either washing his
clothes or fishing. He stated that he saw or heard
blacks between him and the camp, and being on
foot and unarmed made off down the river towards
the nearest place where some other men were
camped. He was overtaken by George with the
horses, who said that when he got back he found
Batten dead, with his skull smashed in. It was
stated that when the native-police went out there
they could find no tracks of natives about the
spot. A black boy of George’s next made a
sudden exit from this world. He had been
barbarously punished by the gentleman in
question, so badly that some white men who
heard of it threatened to report the matter at
once to the police. That night the boy “ ran
away,” at any rate he was never heard or again.
Few who were acquainted with George and the
incidents of his Queensland career, felt much
sympathy with the “execution” of his so-called
murderers.-
CARPENTARIA.
Stand from Under.
Reader ; When you meet a runaway horse, don’t
you “give him room?” When the trams are
coming, don’t you “step aside?” If you see a
falling brick, don’t you “stand from under ? ” Of
course you do ! you are aware of the impending
danger and get out of the way. There are other
dangers though, that you are much more careless
of —you are careless of your health, you abuse
your strength perhaps in one way, perhaps in
another; it, however, all amounts to the same
thing, whenever one draws upon life’s fund so must
the vitality decline ; as the health and strength is
depleted, so must life be shortened, or made
miserable. Take warning when that tired feeling
first comes on, those intense and occasional head-
aches, those aching limbs and sleepless nights,
they are signs that the nervous system is about to
collapse and that danger is at hand, it may be
disease, perhaps insanity, perhapsdeath. Bewarned
in time, tackle the first signs, do not let debility
get a hold on your system or it may be too late.
When nature warns you that disease is about to
attack you do the same as if you saw a chimney
pot descending on your head —Stand from under.
Send to your nearest chemist or storekeeper for
Clements Tonic, and continue its use as long as
there is the slightest sign of any disorder ; don’t
think that one bottle is going to restore strength
that you have been wearing out for years. You
can’t nail patches on the human body like you can
on a barn; you must use proper means to restore
the impaired vitality naturally, and this cannot
be done in a day, but it can be and has been done
in hundreds of severe cases by a proper and con-
tinued use of Clements Tonic, a medicine that can
be depended on to cure every curable case and
that is saying a good deal.
Mr. Thomas Garrett, M.P. (N.S.W.), in speak-
ing of this remedy, says : “He has taken
Clements Tonic with very great advantage.”
Mr. Thos. Walker, M.L.A. (N.S.W.), says:

Clements Tonic as I have derived great benefit

Clements Tonic and am wonderfully improved,
and have no return of the excruciating headaches
since taking it. ”

Mr. and Mrs. Kendal were married while acting in Man-
chester, and had to appear the same night in “As You
Like It,” just as Mr. and Mrs. Chas. Kean acted “ The
Honeymoon ” on their nuptial eve. During the first act
of “As You Like It,” it oozed through the house that
Madge Robertson had married Orlando ; she got a great
call, and when she came out a gallery cupid yelled,
“How d’ye like it?” She archly looked up and ex-
claimed, “I’ll tell you when I next come round to Man-
chester.”. Madge was the youngest of 24 children by one
father and mother.

Notice to Artistic and Literary Contributors
Strictly Original matter contributed to The Bulletin
will be paid for. The Editor will he glad to receive and
consider contribution* a* under : —
1. Original humorous or political matter , illustrated or
otherwise. ( Drawings must be made in clean lines
of Indian ink on white paper or Bristol-board.)
$. Unpublished anecdotes of living celebrities, or people
who ought to be celebrated,
g. Humorous poems (from eight to forty-eight lines pre-
ferred ).
It. Serious poems, of similar or greater length, especially
those suitable for pictorial illustration.
5. Short Australian or other stories, up to, say, SO
words. About half that length, or even less, toil
be preferred.
All Contributions must be Written Legibly, on One
Side Only of the paper, and folded, not rolled. 'I he
name and address of the writer must appear, in all
cases, on the MS. itself, and not merely on an accompany-
ing letter or paper. The Proprietors do not hold them-
selves responsible for Rejected Contributions; but, if
accompanied by a stamped and addressed envelope or
wrapper, every effort will be made to return them.
The Editor does not undertake to enter into any
correspondence respecting rejected Manuscripts.
Insist upon having Wolfe’s Schnapps.
A male Australian has been complaining
in the press concerning the unfitness and un-
suitability of men’s evening dress as worn in these
times. At a recent local large ball he chanced to
accost one of the invited guests as “ vyaitah !” lie
advocates as a preventive against similar mistakes
in the future some change being made in the cos-
tume of either the master or his servants, adding
that “a crying abuse of this kind need on'y to be
thoroughly realised in order to meet with the
necessary reform.” No doubt it must greatly im-
pair the amour propre of those useless persons
who happen to be mistaken for members of the
operative classes, but as the man who presumes on
the poverty of his fellow men to impose tasks
upon them which he would refuse to return in
kind, is simply a callous slave-driver, such injured
feelings are really of small account, after all.
When Pere Loti, on the occasion of his servants’
marriage, kindly gave the wedding party the use
of his mansion for the nonce and personally
attended on the bride and bridegroom, he struck
a chord which powerfully reverberated in the
affections and respect of his faithful servitors, and,
given a few more Pere Lotis’, the cause of Socialism
would be seriously retarded. To some it may seem
unaccountable that a waiter should differ extrin-
sically in no wise from a millionaire, and more so
that viewed intrinsically the menial should
display far higher quantities than his master.
To remedy this unfortunate circumstance, which
has so subversive a tendency towards the old order
of things, society proposes to compel the lacquey
to don a livery or else to place a label on the breast-
plate of the boss, in order that mistakes or ‘‘cry-
ing abuses” need not occur again. Vain and
foolish, though you paint the host blue and the
attendant yellow for discriminative purposes the
fact will ever remain that the servant is as good as
his master, and just now when the current of
events is drifting rapidly towards a point where
all social anomalies will fuse, it behoves those
possessed of exceptional privileges to keep a
watchful eye on their words and actions, and to use
their advantages tenderly, and nowhere presume,
otherwise the climax may be hurried and convulsive
instead of peaceful. The more accentuated the lines
of demarcation between the classes and the masses
the greater the indignation and animosity
engendered. It appears, therefore, advisable to
drop the dress-suit and livery business, and tp
remember in the future that if a guest should be
taken for a w r aiter, the compliment is, if anything,
on the side of the former. An ex-Duke of France
has filled the function of at a common
restaurant, and one of the greatest scoundrels
unhung posed as host to Napoleon 111, amongst
whose menage the most depraved waiter was im-
measurably his superior.

St. Petersburg, is said to have concluded a
brilliant lecture on the use and abuse of acids by
pouring a few drops into a phial, and requesting
his class of students to remain for two minutes
and see a man die. Whereupon he swallowed the
contents of the phial, counted two minutes by his
gold repeater, and died according to promise. The
students remained “speechless and motionless”
whilst the tragedy was enacted, and the professor

Here endeth the acid drops story, wlrch is
apocryphal on the face of it. A man of that
description would only take poison for love or
jealousy, and then he would take it in the
woman’s presence, with a view to making her feel
creepy. Unless, indeed, he was utterly tired of

not have further worried himself by the delivery

Wolfe’s Schnapps—It is grand.
“ Revivalism,” deposed Coroner Youl in
the Kits case, “ is certainly a sign of insanity.”
At the recent “reconciliation ” service in
St. Paul’s, London, which was intended to purge
that majestic tabernacle from the after-effects of
somebody’s suicide, the Bishop said “they knew
not whether any sin of the deceased contributed
to the confusion of brain which ended in his des-
troying himself, but they were all miserable sin
ners and ought to ask the Almighty to look favour-
ably upon their worship so that they might feel
the more deeply what was the meaning of that
mysterious evil which every now and then showed
itself in such acts as this.” No medical man
happened to be present, perhaps, or he would have
crushed the Bishop with the information that
religion is accountable for as much of the world’s
insanity as rum.

JPJRITONS IN NYASS ALAND.
SOUTH-EAST AFRICA.
Livingstone, Lake Nyassa, East Central Africa,
Karonga,
26th Feb., 1889.
Gentlemen, —At the urgent request of a friend
in Africa, I have been persuaded to write you
regarding one of the Waterbury Watches, which
I wear every day, and can recommend for time-
keeping ability. It has been in Africa for three
years, travelled from London to Zanzibar, from
Zanzibar to Lake Tanganyika, from Lake Tan-
ganyika to Lake Nyassa. It has been twice under
water in African rivers, had at least two falls,
and been severely handled by many natives, many
of whom look upon it as something marvellous.
Once it was taken down when the spring leaped
from the case, giving us all a fright, and lay some
yards off, a giant thing of many feet in length.
In the fights we have had at Nyassa with the
Arabs, it has been with me in several engage-
ments. Notwithstanding all it keeps good time,
and it is not rivalled by yaluable watches. For
wear and tear and time-keeping in a rough country
like Africa, I think th c re is nothing better.
Believe me, gentlemen, most respectfully yours,
David Kewe Cross, M.8.C.M.,
Ordained Missionary, Livingstonia.
The Waterbury Watch Co.
P.S.—The watch is one of your humblest.—
D.K.C.
CAUTlON.—lntending purchasers of the
genuine article should note that every watch
manufactured by the Waterbury Watch Co., has
the word “WATERBURY ” printed in fine type
upon the dial-face, and also the interlaced letters
W. W. Co., without which none is genuine.

“ During his residence in this colony we have had full
opportunity of making Mr. Raymond’s acquaintance, and
of observing the cures he has performed, and we have no
hesitation in saying that in our opinion he is a genuine
man and a skilled physician. We have come personally
into contact with a number of pel-sons that have been
cured by him of eye and ear diseases of a complicated and
difficult character, so that we speak from actual know-
ledge and observation. Professor Raymond is a botanic
physician ; he uses no mineral drugs, and his treatment is
painless and uninjurious.”— The Christian Colonist,
Adelaide, 1889.
The FOLLOWING CURE that has just been EFFECTED
in SYDNEY not only supports what has been done else-
where, but illustrates the superiority of Dr. RAYMOND’S
method of combating difficult and complicated affections
of the Human ear, ,
“To Dr. RAYMOND, Aurist, Oculist, and Botanic
Physician. . T , . ,
“Dear Sir, —In response to your request, I desire to
publicly testify to the benefit I have received from your
treatment. You have considerably improved my hearing
and general health, although I had previously been under
the treatment of one doctor in Sydney, and been in con-
sultation with several others. I had also used Nicholson's
ear drums, lotion and nose powder, and without receiving
the slightest benefit therefrom. You have treated my
case, nose and throat deafness, botanically, and, in my
opinion, your system is the right one—you, at the same
time attacking the root of the disease with internal treat-
ment. I shall be happy to answer any enquiries as to the
benefit I have received whilst under your treatment.—
Yours, very truly. H. TAYLOR, Palisade Hotel, Millers
Point,’November 14th, 1890.”

To H. F. Kugei.mann, Esq.
Dear Sir,—This is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, which
turned into Bronchitis and became chronic. / consulted
more than one doctor, and during that time took a great
quantity of medicine without it doing me the slightest
good, but I became worse every day, wasting atony until
I became a perfect skeleton. About February last I began
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and severe
pains in my left lung, and the quantity of bad matter I
was expectorating every dayq I became very much alarmed.
Hearing of your professional skill 1 consulted you, and
you told me you could cure me for a given sum. I agreed
and went under your treatment in March last. At the
end of three months I was taken bad one morning, and
vomited about two pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. From that time I commenced to improve, and
was gaining in weight every day. About a month ago the
blood ceased coming, the cough disappeared, and the
king healed completely, and thank God and your pro-
fessional skill, I am as well as ever.
P. KEENAN.
Declared and subscribed before me, one of Her Majesty’s
Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick, in the
County of Grant, this 10th day of December, 1888.
Peter S.mytiie, J.P.

Attention is directed to the prospectus of
the Metropolitan Mont de Pie to Deposit and
Investment Co., Ltd., now being floated with the
laudable intention of doing a legitimate business
at reasonable rates. Considering the exorbitant
charges made by individuals, the new company
deserves the support which has already been
accorded it. So great has been the demand for
shares, that it is anticipated they will cniy he
obtainable at a premium in future.*
