The Referee
There must be something radically wrong when
a province which raises the finest professional scullers in
the world cannot get together an amateur crew able to
extend, let alone' beat, crews from the sister pro-
vinces. Mismanaging everything from the start, the
Sydney people evidently thought they might as well be
consistent to the end, so they put off the conveying of
their boat to the starting-point till the afternoon, and as
it was then blowing a westerly gale it had to be tovved up
stream, while Victoria and Queensland sat waiting in
their boats for over an hour—extraordinary treatment to
extend to one’s guests. However, it was scarcely worth
delaying the race, as Sydney made but a sorry show.
Again The Bulletin gives its opinion that it would be
better for N.S.W. to stand out than thus to be held up to
ridicule year after year.
# * *
Queensland had hard luck. When approaching
One Man Wharf they had a commanding lead, and though
Victoria, which steadily decreased the gap, was going
magnificently and would certainly have pushed them lo
half a boat’s'length, it looked like the Bananamen’s race
until No. 2 (W. B. Molle) broke an oar, thus demoralising
the crew and giving the Gumsuckers first position. The
history of one of the Oxford and Cambridge races, and,
later, of a Harvard and Yale race, was repeated, inasmuch
as Molle jumped out of the boat; but there the similarity
ends, as the seven oars won in both the British and
American University races, whilst on Saturday Queens-
land could not make up lost ground, Victoria winning by
some four lengths. Sydney was distanced, and it is hard
to say how many men would have had to jump out of the
other boats to give Mr. Goodyear’s extraordinary crew a
chance of holding them.
# * *
A daily-newspaper correspondent avers that no
serious result has ever followed a prize-fight between two
trained men, and that a doctor tells him that a good,
sound fbdit is good for the constitutions of both com-
batants. ° Lovely joke ! Not only have dozens of trained
men been killed in prize-fights, but hundreds of men,
who have not been killed outright, have received such
serious injuries as to render them practically of no further
use in this world.
* * *
There is no doubt that a strong official move-
ment has now been initiated in the direction of putting
down the prize-fights thinly veiled in the guise of glove-
contests to a finish. With the toned-down mills the
patrons of prize-fighting will not be satisfied. Australia
is now, The Bulletin predicts, on the verge of a revival
of the raw-meat-and-grass battle of the old-time English
turf-the secret, early-morning gathering a few miles out
of town, the hurriedly-pitched ring, the big scoot in 500
different directions at the approach of the police, the
arrest of the aiders and abettors, and the practical
decease of knock-out fighting.
* # *
During the prevailing anti-boxing crusade, the
Sydney Amateur Athletic Club could not have had a
worse man than Burge to fight at their hall. Devoid of
science, and slow as a top, he relies upon his wonderful
powers of taking punishment to wear-down more scientific
opponents, whose hands are usually knocked into pulp
upon Ironbark' Jimmy’s cast-iron nob. The gory disfigure-
ment of Burge’s frontispiece certainly resolved the
fbdit into a sickening and brutal spectacle, and the
referee showed much want of acumen as well as of
humanity in giving the police a chance to interfere. Not
that Burge was “ knocked out oh, dear, no ! There is
an old story to the effect that the iron man, when work-
in"- on the Bondi sewer, fell down a 30ft. shaft, and
then cursed heartily at having broken a favourite pipe.
* * *
Notwithstanding Burge’s partial blindness, the
writer does not think Dawson would have been able to
knock him right out. Bather early in the fight Dawson
used his right-hand up, and towards the last he was
naturally weakening fast, and, blind or no blind, it is
hard to say how many times Burge might have gone
down before Dawson’s left, taken his 10 seconds, and then
stood up for more. Burge, however, long before the
fbdit finished, had received such horrible punishment
that the referee would, under the new rule, have been
justified in awarding the flsrht to Dawson.
# * *
However, as the men were allowed to go on
until the poboe interfered, it was manifestly unfair to
Burge to give the fight to Dawson. It was not a light
to be decided on “points.” It was “ to a finish,” or
unt'l the referee considered one of the men not in a fit
condition to go on. He did notconsider Burge beaten, or
he would not have awaited police interference, so the
contest should, without a doubt, have been declared a
“ draw.”
# # •
If the Burge-Dawson affair does not put an end
to the Sydney boxing boom, it is to be hoped that the
“iron man” will not again be pitted against a strong,
determined and scientific boxer. The Sydney club is
ostensibly run only in the interests of athleticism, and
should not again allow mere butchering in its ring, when
so many scientific men are at hand.
# * *
The earliest record of a prize-fight in Queens-
land describes a mill between Jim Smith, the “Native,”
and “ Black Bill,” who fought barehanded for £5O a side
at Ipswich on the 29th February, 1818. These festive
warriors of the olden days disposed of 32 rounds in 42
minutes, and then the nigger was knocked out by a right
hander on the jugular. In the previous year, 1547, Isaac
Reid, known as “ Big Ike,” who had fought Hough, the
“ Parramatta Native,” for the championship of N.S.W.,
died at Yass on Ist December.
* * *
Selling races are ever unsatisfactory items, and
one at a recent Sydney suburban meeting proved no
exception to the rule. A horse was backed heavily by the
public, but he made such a poor show against the
moderate field opposed to him that the crowd loudly
showed their disapproval upon his return to the paddock.
One backer had the temerity to say he would “claim”
the horse, when he was duly pounced upon and received a
drubbing from a well known bully who is a regular
attendant at the races in and about Sydney. Selling
races are put upon the programmes and are run under
rules which state any horse in the race may be “claimed,”
but it is an open secret that the man who “claims” a
horse belonging to at least one stable, does so at the risk
of a thrashing. An outsider would think t hat various
clubs, in their own financial interests, would put this sort
of‘thing down with an iron hand. -
* * *
At the recent Brisbane race-meeting the top-
rails of the hurdles were all but sawn through on the
night before the races. This considerate attempt to let
the “ crocks ” down light was upset by the substitution
of sound rails ere the event came off.
# # , «
The A.J.O. would do well to pass a rule
prohibiting trainers from holding an interest in pro-
prietary courses. It would cleanse the sport a lot.
Various are the tricks of the trade. At Canter-
bury, on Saturday, Giltinan, with Hugo's colors on,
paraded the paddock before the horses went out for the
Welter, and Hugo’s price was 4to 1. When the board
went up it was seen “ Bill ” Kelso had the mount, and the
public rushed him down to 6to 4. He vion handily,

The Sydney Ladies’ Cricket Club intend to play
a match on the Association Cricket Ground on 26th and
27th December. A match is also contemplated with an
eleven of the Melbourne Ladies’ Club, which has now
upwards of 40 members.
* * #
A Melbourne paper asserts that the “lady”
cricketers of that town live all together in a four-roomed
cottage, and send out regularly for beer. Their male
brethren think the latter beverage indispensable.
# * *
“Tommy Japan,” of Thursday Island, who
recently won £22,000 by a lucky draw in Adams’ sweep,
began by keeping a small hash-house for the accommoda-
tion of coloured divers engaged in melting their cheques.
Now, Thursday Island desires to testify to Tommy’s
worth by making him a J.P.
# « *
Geelong Racing Club is playing the “ sport of
kings ” down about as low as it will go. The other Satur-
day’s programme included a trot, a pony handicap, and a
pony selling race, and £75 was the largest stake that an
owner could pick up at one fell swoop—a small sum indeed
to recompense any runner for the indignity of being
solemnly cursed from 75 Geelong pulpits next morning.
Until the G.R.C. institutes a selling race for parsons and
gets somebody to “ claim ” the whole lot of runners and
turn them out to grass, sport can never flourish in the
original capital of Victoria.
* * *
It is said that W. G. Rowdon’s high jump of
6ft. Sin. was made from a small spring block raised 7in.
at the broad end. Rowdon has lost his amateur status
through demanding payment for his exhibitions.
# # *
Two men, a professional and a novice, fought
63 rounds the other day at M‘Donald's Creek over a girl,
it ended in a draw, and the lady is now walking out with
ai other gentleman, the bridge of whose nose is in its
pristine condition, and whose unsplit lips remain as yet
red and kissable.

Odi Profanum.
Dear Bulletin,— The Latin title of this little
article may be - translated “Avaunt, ye un-
washed 1” I have chosen it advisedly. Ever
since I can remember distinct thought I
have been an iconoclast, an anarchist, a dis-
ciple of Brad laugh before he was respectable,
and of Mrs. Besant before she espoused the doc-
trines of esoteric Buddhism. Yet, along with
active hatred of the average duke, the wealthy
retired contractor, the bishop, I find in my heart
an even more deeply-rooted aversion and con-
tempt for the average cabman, shopman, shearer,
and banker’s clerk. It remains to be seen whether
this is merely an objectionable peculiarity of my
own, or whether really I belong to a large school
of thinkers who have never as yet confessed their
beliefs, but who will now recognise me as their
mouthpiece.
* # *
We will assume for the present that my duke,
with his insolent opulence, his crowd of related
flunkeys, his inevitable entanglements with low
opera-girls, his hereditary diseases and his natural
inaccessibility to ideas, is practically doomed.
The loner-delayed vengeance, merited by the long
series of offences perpetrated by ancestors whom
he does not disclaim, is about to burst on his head.
At any rate, no one will want to quarrel with me
because a duke disgusts and irritates me. We
will also assume that all the sympathy with the
working-man, so commonly expressed by poets and
philanthropists, is genuine. Chaucer, Milton,
Shakespeare, and Shelley, have clasped him to
their heart. I alone regard him with aversion and
contempt. Is it possible to explain this, and to
prove that my attitude is exactly the proper one ?
* * #
Let us examine first what the real grievance of
Labour is. So far as I can form a distinct mental
picture of society in England and India now, in
Greece and Egypt long ago, I see always the down-
trodden millions incessantly producing, and inces-
santly robbed. Whether a few priests and soldiers,
a few brilliant statesmen, a few alien usurpers, or
a few capitalists held the reins of power, it was
always the same thing. The people who ought to
have acted as captains of labour, organising it so
that enough should be produced, and regulating
the distribution so that each should have an equal
share, invariably allowed the energies of the
workers to be dissipated and ineffective, and con-
tented themselves with as much of the produce as
they could appropriate without causing absolute
revolution. Here we see the radical flaw in the
argument of ordinary agitators. They con-
tinually asseverate that if the drones were all
killed the working bees would have each a fair
share of the honey. They do not see that, the
drones being few, their wealth would, when
divided, be but a trifle for each worker.* The
crime of the ruling classes is not primarily that
they steal, but that they do not rule. '
* * #
So far, I think careful readers will allow that
Chaucer, Milton, Shakespeare and Shelley are on
my side. Why is it then that they manifest love
and sympathy, while I feel only contempt ? The
answer is easy enough when you hear it. To their
great minds, the ideal and typical was real and
tangible. _ They saw Demos like a Samson
struggling in his chains. They pitied him for his
wrongs, and loved him as the strong love the weak.
Where they saw the general, I see the particular.
I meet bricklayers, shearers, cabmen, woodcutters,
and I find them as a rule besottedly ignorant,
grossly selfish, personally so dirty that they smell,
cruel in their pastimes, servile to the strong,
brutal to the weak. i‘ All their ideals are false.
They admire, hate, love, laugh, cry, exactly when
they should on no account do so. This accounts
for the harshness with which they are treated by
the upper-classes generally. The “ gentry ” know
that no working man will ever thank them for
kindness, and they extend to them something less
than justice.
* * *
What, then, is the political platform of one like
myself, who is an extremist in Radicalism and at
the same time full of contempt for the working-
man ? To reform him, of course. What he wants
is education, money, leisure. When he has those
I shall no longer feel aversion for him. Nobody
can be a true champion of labour who does not
feel as keenly as I do the reason why the working
man needs a helping hand. And the reason, I
have shown, is that ages of servitude have
developed the servile vices. If he were clean, wise,
unselfish, gentle, manly, he might well be allowed
to remain poor. The reason poverty is a curse is
that it inevitably produces, sooner or later, the
low moral type I have described before.
• * #
There is just now an election going on in New
Zealand, and never having sat in Parliament, it
occurred to me it would be amusing to contest a
constituency. It was clear that in order to get
the labour-vote it would be necessary to explain
to the electors why Ofle who despises them in-
dividually, as much as I do, for the fact is well
known, is really the only person likely to make a
serious attempt to raise theto from servitude with
its vices to freedom with its Virtues. Any agitator
who tells the labouring classes that they are not,
in their present condition, fit objects for contempt,
does not realise why poverty is an evil. Were
any reasonable amount of virtue compatible with
incessant drudgery, they might well be allowed to
drudge on. Their only real claim to more money,
more education, more leisure, is that existing ser-
vile vices , can only so be dispelled. But the
electors in my district have a firm idea that the
test of a candidate is whether he will shout them
long beers. They would not understand a
word of what I have expressed briefly, but
I hope clearly, above, and, therefore, I have
turned to a more rational audience and
endeavoured to explain my idea to your
readers. Tor in a country electorate the
proportion of intelligent voters is small, whereas
in Sydney hundreds of men who read these lines
will agree with me. Indeed, lam Pot quite sure
that you will be wise in publishing this. As like
as not, a body of roughs will break your windows
for allowing me to say that “ down-trodden serfs ”
do not inherit gentlemanly instincts. I am quite
convinced, however, that whenever the real
reformer arises he will speak as frankly as I do,
and I believe a number of really humane and
honorable men will thank me for having expressed
in plain terms thoughts which they were dimly
conscious of, but had not elaborated with sufficient
precision to express in writing or even in speech.
W.F. HOWLETT.
Makaretu, Waipawa, New Zealand.
[We publish the above gay and festive epistle for several
reasons. Among others, because we would like tolcombat
the excessively stale and feeble argument {vide *) that a
fairer division of the world’s wealth would not much in-
crease the earnings of labour. If it be taken into
consideration that all estimates as to the world’s wealth
are calculated per head, it will be seen that in the case of
the husband of a wife and the father of a family, the
yearly increase would not be so insignificant. Then, the
indirect increase must not be forgotten. The chief objec-
tion to competitive production and monopoly is that
they limit production. The communisation of all the
sources of wealth would immensely increase the world’s
annual production; this increase of production, com-
bined with absolute fairness in distribution, would surely
entirely annihilate pauperism, if not poverty. Even if the
workers were, as our correspondent asserts, unclean, sel-
fish, ungrateful, and ignorant brutes,f these vices could
not be inherent, must be the result of environment, and
therefore their existence would only, as the apostle of
Tirenism, to do him justice, asserts, furnish another
powerful argument for social reconstruction.—Ed.B.]

A Californian “oyster-cocktail” consists
of a few small fat oysters placed in a tumbler,
Which is then filled with a mixtureof salt, pepper,
lime-juice, Worcestershire sauce, and oyster liquor.
No one needs to be told how to take it. It will
wake the dying to life—if you put in enough
Worcester.

Brigand Stanley has no alternative hut to
maintain that Major Barttelot was mad, seeitlg
that the dead man openly expressed his fears that
Stanley would poison all his “staff,” so as to be
able to pose as the sole surviving leader of his
ghastly expedition. Anyway, it is evident that
Stanley was not much of a success in a missionary
sense. To clear his own character he must pro-
duce a witness who will swear that two of the
“ hero’s ” colleagues amused themselves by kicking
boys to death and buying girls for the purpose of
seeing them eaten alive, and taking authentic
sketches of the process. Stanley will have to ex-
plain why he didn’t publish those sketches with
his faithful account of all that transpired in
Darkest Africa. We shall never get the true
history of that tropical picnic until Dolly Tennant
lets the world into the secret of her husband’s
nightmare babblings after suppers of cold pork
and cucumber. One of these fine mornings Dolly
will look in the glass and discover that her hair is
as white as that of the pirate book-canvasser, her
husband.

Mortgage, Discount, and Deposit Bank, of 4
Moore-street, Sydney, has recently been incor-
porated, and is now opened for business. This
bank has been formed for the purpose of assisting
those who may at any time be in pecuniary diffi-
culties and wish to avoid paying the excessive in-
terest which is too often charged. The bank has
secured the able services of Mr. Henry A. Birk-
myre, which should be a guarantee that clients will
be fairly dealt with. In these days of extortion, the
above institution should about fill a long-felt
want.*

Tales of “The System.”
No. XXI.—Convict Rudde’s Proposal of
Marriage.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Henry Rudde, No. 157, transport per Lord
Lynedoch, and assigned man to E. A. Slyde,
Esq., Police Magistrate of Sydney, was in the
seventh heaven of bliss. His master had just
granted him permission to marry. His joy was
the more intense, in that it had been preceded by
a shock of fear.
# * *
Mr. Rudde was overseer at the P.M.’s
Parramatta Farm, and was consequently in a
position of some glory and much responsibility,
though slight emolument. Still, he was not
altogether comfortable. He made a considerable
amount of money for his master at the useful, if
unromantic occupation of growing cabbages, but
he was anxious to make some for himself. Un-
fortunately, one little obstacle impeded the way
to the fulfilment of that laudable wish. He was a
“lifer,” and, therefore, not eligible for a ticket-
of-leave till 12 years had .elapsed since his convic-
tion. At’the moment when we confer upon Mr.
Rudde the honour of introducing him to Bulletin
readers, five years had still to pass before he
could enjoy the fruits (and vegetables) of quasi-
independence. And, as he saw his manhood’s
prime gradually slipping away from him, and his
capacity for minting gold out of the white hearts
of cabbages being put to no other use than to
swell Mr. Slyde’s bank - account, his temper
soured somewhat and Ins energies flagged—a
couple of facts which, it is perhaps needless to
say, did not escape the keen-eyed P. M.
Mr. Slyde had been paying one of his customary
Sabbath visits to his farm, and had detected
numerous systems of a relaxed vigilance. A
straggling hedge, an unrepaired fence, a hingeless
door and a cart-wheel in an advanced state of
wobblmess were significant to the experienced
disciplinarian and admirable Administrator of a
turn for the worse in Rudde’s management.
“ Rudde,” he said, as he entered the homestead,
after his inspection was ended for the day;
“ Rudde, you’re getting sulky ! ”
The assigned man started and trembled. Mr.
Slyde’s voice conveyed a subtle menace. No
convict ever excited Mr. Slyde’s anger without
suffering for it in body and mind. Rudde w r as well
aware of this interesting fact,, and hence suffered
in anticipation.
“Rudde,’’proceeded Mr.Slyde, “sulkinessdon’t
pay with me! You’ll have to alter that game, my
man. One bad report from me, and —phew!—
your chance of a ticket is blown sky-high.”
“ I ain’t sulky, sir,” stammered Rudde.
“ You are, sir ! Don’t lie ! If there’s a thing
I hate it’s a lie ! I know a lie when I hear one,
and you’re lying now !”
The enemies of the Assignment System founded
their principal objections to it on the impossibility
of ensuring to the assigned servants good example
and moral precept on the part of the employers.
It is obvious that if all masters and mistresses had
shared Mr. Slyde’s repugnance to a lie, the ob-
jection would have been deprived of all weight.
“ Well, sir, I’m a-gettin’ a bit down-’arted, sir.
It’s not as if I were a workin’ for myself, sir.”
The P.M. assumed his best dehortatory coun-
tenance as he replied to this astonishing outbreak.
“ Working for yourself ? Do you forget what you
are, Rudde—a transport, a convict, a prisoner of
the Crown ? You, working for yourself ! Have
you forgotten that if you had had your deserts,
you would have been hanged, and instead of being
as you are now my assigned servant and alive, you
would have been a skeleton with a dislocated neck.
» Working for yourself!—the idea.”
“But, sir, meanin’ no ’arm, sir,” whimpered
poor Rudde, who already felt his flesh creeping
under the “cat,” “other lifers who kem,. in the
same ship, sir, are a-workin’ for themselves.”
“ What!” thundered Slyde, jealous of the dignity
of the King’s Regulations—“ what! lifers made at
the same time as you got their tickets already ? I
don’t believe it ! I’ve already warned you not to
tell me lies !”
“ Tis true, sir, ’tis! There’s Goodyear and
Renny, sir!”
“Ah, I’ll see to that,” said his master, as he
proceeded to note in his pocket-book the names of
the men in whose favour the Regulations had been
so flagrantly set aside; “to whom were those
fellows assigned, d’ye know?”
‘ * Goodyear to Mr. Macleay, sir, and Renny to
Sir John Jamieson, sir.”
“Um—ah!” And Mr. Slyde shut his book
with a snap. There was nothing to be made by
accusing gentlemen of high stations of violating
the Regulations. He could do no more than men-
tally curse them for setting so bad an example.
The System was going to the devil, he thought,
when the very rich men of the colony were so
squeamishly soft as to permit their assigned hands
to work for themselves before their tickets were
properly due. The news, too, made him reflect a
minute before further talking to his overseer. It
was a confoundedly unfortunate coincidence that
the wretches so enfranchised by Macleay and
Jamieson should be “Lord Lynedoch lifers ” as
well as his own men. The circumstance could but
provoke comparisons, and Slyde was not so well
beloved of his brother Administrators as to pre-
clude the comparisons being made in such a way
as to prove odious and odorous. Therefore,
upon reflection, he determined to be magnanimous,
and not to punish Rudde for daring to assert a
wish for independence and the filthy lucre which
the least industrious of free or ticket-of-leave men
could scarcely avoid picking up in those days.
“Well, Rudde,” he said, at last, “I’ll let you
oft this time.” (How Rudde thanked his stars ! he
had quite made'up his mind to “fifty.”) “But
don’t try it on again—and don’t neglect your
work or I’ll really have to get your ticket stopped.
And, now, just to encourage you, and show you I
mean you to get on, I’ll tell you what I’ll do.”
He infused a genial kindliness into his tones
that made his assigned man pinch himself to make
sure he wasn’t dreaming.
“I’ll tell you what I’ll do,” Slyde repeated,
“I’ll allow you to get married !”
“ Married, sir ! ” gasped the fellow, thus
honoured beyond his deservings. “Married !”
“Yes. Oorne inside, and I’ll give you an order
~*v Mrs. Gordon for a wife.”
* * *
Rudde followed his master into the home-
stead, and emerged, a few minutes later, with two
slips of paper in his hand, and a look of wonder-
ment on his face. One of the slips was a pass
which permitted him to be absent for three days
from his master’s holding—an ample period, Mr.
Slyde considered, for Eudde to make the acquaint-
ance of his future spouse, get the knot tied, and
spend the honey-time—and the other was an
order to obtain the lady in question from the
Parramatta Factory.
As Mr. Slyde seated himself at bis table in the
parlour, to write these valuable documents, he
proceeded to demonstrate to his astounded over-
seer how the new arrangement would facilitate
his, Rudde’s, desire to earn money. ,
“I’ll get this place proclaimed as a pound,.
Rudde, and on my recommendation you’ll get the
post of pound-keeper. Now, if you just work it
righj; there’s a lot of money in that business. By
the time you’re due for your ticket you ought to
be worth three or four hundred pounds. Do you
understand ?”
Mr. Rudde, having been seven years a resident
in the colony, and having, moreover, kept his
eyes pretty wide open to the various ways by
which certain prominent settlers had made money,
intimated he did understand—perfectly !
“That’s right,” continued Mr. Slyde. “Now,
of course, I can’t afford to let you devote a lot of
your time to the pound without some compen-
sation ?”
Mr. Rudde nodded a respectful assent.
“Then we’ll agree that you should pay me £2O
a year rental for the impounding-paddock.”
..Again Mr. Rudde assented.
“ That’s right again. Now we come to the next
point. You see you’ll be away a good deal looking
after stock to impound, and somebody must be
here to keep an eye on the other assigned hands,
and as I don’t want another overseer about the
place—you wouldn’t like that either, would you ?
—I think you’d better be married right off. A
decent sort of woman would look after you and
the hands, too, and, besides, Mrs. Slyde and I are
coming up here once or twice in the week in
future : the change from the town does us good,
and it’ll be more comfortable for us that a woman
should be in the house to keep things tidy. You
understand that?”
Rudde’s acquiescence this time was more
measured. “ Y-e-es !” he managed to say, after a
hesitancy which should have made clear to Mx.
Slyde the true cause of his unwillingness to
receive a bride on the instant. Perhaps Mr.
Slyde did apprehend, but, if so, it was not his
business to indicate that he possessed any insight
into the difficulties of Rudde’s position.
“Y-e-es !” he echoed. “Well, ’pon my word !
you don’t appear very anxious to be spliced,
Rudde? But you’re a fool. And it’s the only
condition on which I’ll retain you here as over-
seer. You’ll have to work under another man,
else. You won’t like that, after being a sort of
master here. You can’t make money unless you
have the pound, and you shan’t have the pound
unless you marry.- And I can’t have things going
to.wreck and ruin because you’re discontented;
and if you won’t marry, and so earn money for
yourself, I can see you’ll soon be useless as over-
seer.”
“But, Mr. Slyde—sir—l can’t marry. I am—
married ! I was married afore I was-Jagged, ”
was Rudde’s hard-breathed response,
The P.M. laughed. “You are a d d fool,
Rudde. Aou mean you were married !”
“ Yes, I was married, sir, and my wife was
’live w’en I last ’erd, sir.”
“ Just so,” said Mr. Slyde. “ And you last heard
about a year ago?” For the few letters which
formed Rudde’s correspondence pas»«d through
Mr. Slyde(s hands,
“ Yes, sir, full a year ago !”
“ Oh, well, don’t bother about that. You’ll get
another letter to-morrow—l’ll send it by old
Pounce—telling you of her death. Keep it care-
fully, you know, so if anything happens you’ll get
off because you married the second time under a
wrong impression. And you’d better give me one
of your old letters—l’ll take care the stamp’s fixed
up all right.”
His employer’s willingness to smooth over the
difficulties connected with entrance upon fresh
connubial obligations removed Overseer Rudde’s
last objection to the proposed union, and as he
went out from Mr. Slyde’s presence with the two
precious documents already referred to in his
hands, his heart was freed from its canker of dis-
content, and his spirits mounted joyously. The
more he contemplated the prospect before him,
the happier he felt, and after a night’s sleepless
tossing on his soon-to-be-no-longer-widowed bed,
he rose, fully convinced of Mr. Slyde’s generosity,
and of his own superlative good fortune,
“Why,” he said to himself, as he ascertained
his wedding morning was balmy and sunshiny,
“I ain’t felt so ’appy since I was a boy. I warn’t
’arf so spry when I was married to Polly—not by
long chalks.” And, as he harrowed his locks with
the unwonted comb he completely forgot his
chains, and mounted in imagination to the high
heavens of felicity. In fancy, he saw_ himself a
free man, and (thanks to the pound and its delicate
function as a wealth-producer) a rich old squatter,
and the founder of a “Family.” Even in these
early days, men talked of founding a colonial
aristocracy.
« • #
,
Shortly before noon, Pounce, the forger,
arrived at Slyde’s Farm from the city. A most
useful man w’as Pounce. The System used him;
its Administrators availed themselves of his ex-
pertness with the pen and the erasing-knife ; and
he carried on, in addition, a thriving trade with
the “ Gov’ment-men.” His stately and venerable
presence shrouded a soul deep-dyed in criminality,
and an intellect versed in every devious device for
getting round a moral principle, or through
an Act of Parliament. Transported (after
reprieve) for forgery of bank-stock, he had already
been constructively hanged in Sydney for placing
Governor Darling’s name to a Conditional
Pardon. But he was too useful a man to be
hanged absolutely. The System could have
better spared a better man.
Was it necessary (in the conjoined interests of
a contractor and a Treasury-clerk) to alter the
terms of contract after the agreement had been
passed by the Executive Council? Old Pounce’s
faculty was ready to effect the alteration,
Was there an awkward discrepancy between a
deed of land-grant and the register in the Survey
Office, who was there other than old Pounce who
could be trusted to furnish the requisite deftness
of touch to scrape the parchments and to write-in
the substituted entries?
When Jasker—convict just liberated—brutally
and ungratefully assaulted Mr. Robert Warpby,
of the Civil Service—had not Warphy used his

influence to procure Jasker’s release ?*—and
Jasker had been ordered three hundred lashes,
who was it, when the prisoner died under the
seventeenth stroke, and it was then found that no
doctor was present, altered the record of the
sentence to “fifty ” lashes?f Why, Pounce, to be
sure.
And when, as not unfrequently happened, the
System or its administrators required a convict’s
; -“ ora f letters to be manipulated, or a new
letter to be inscribed within a sheet bearing an
old post-mark, there was no one like Pounce to
bring about the desired end.
No wonder, then, that Mr. Pounce was too
highly esteemed by the System for it to hang him
yeiawhile. Though, to tell the whole truth and
? J'he truth, no transport that ever
landed on these shores would have so appro-
priately adorned the gibbet on Pinohgut as this
worthy scrivener. Thomas Griffiths Wainwright,
John Hardy Vaux, Savory and Fletcher, these
were children beside him.
* * *
Rudde knew Mr. Pounce well. He had once
upon a time solicited the “ penman’s ” talents in
his own behalf, and, wishing to make a bolt up-
country, had wished to purchase from Mr. Pounce
a pass or certificate of freedom.
N°w, if there was one thing more than another
on Which Mr. Pounce plumed himself, it was his
capacity to successfully prepare a pardon, a ticket
or a pass to suit any description of person. H«
had always on hand a considerable number of
blanks’—obtained no one could say how—and
also numerous certificates purchased from the
men and women properly entitled to them, which
he would change as occasion served. Where ho
c , ou 'd nob alter a pardon, he altered the person.
As a skilful tatooist, Pounce, if not supreme, was
still eminent among Sydney practitioners.
Nevertheless, in spite of his unquestioned ac-
complishment in the art of granting Freedom
(for a substantial monetary consideration) to con-
victs froin whom a partial Administration withheld
Liberty, Mr. Pounce would not entertain Rudde’s
proposal. He assiired Rudde that his hand had
lost his cunning, the objection to complying with
the request being, however, Mr. Pounce’s reluct-
lance to annoy Slyde. For Slyde was his most
remunerative client. Nobody gave him so many
little jobs of forg—hush, we mean so many
‘ corrections ’’—as Mr. Slyde. And he wasn’t
going to offend Slyde for the ten-pound note which
Rudde would pay him.
* * #
Rudde and Pounce, therefore, when they met
saluted each other as old acquaintances.
As-the latter descended from the caravan on the
road-gate near the homestead, Rudde welcomed
him.
“Quite well, Mr. Pounce? ’Ope you’re better,
sir. You must be since you ’sumed bis’ness, sir.”
. Ah, how do, Rudde ? Hope I see you well, on
this auspicious day ? Yes, I’m better—just able
to do a little to oblige my friends.” 4
“ Glad to ’ear it, sir. You’ve got somethin’ for
me, I suppose ?”
Yes, Rudde an Fnglish letter, from your
wife s mother, Rudde, I believe, informing you,
I’m sorry to say, of the untimely decease of your
late lamented partner. Mr. Slyde gave me the
letter for you. ” And he handed Rudde a folded
paper.
“Wife’s mother?” said Rudde. “My ol’
■’©Oman’s mother turned up ’er toes afore I was
lagged !”
“Indeed !” said Pounce, surprised. ‘‘l don't
think I made a mistake. I’m sure Mr. Slyde said
it was your wife’s mother the letter was to come
from!”
Miy wife s brother , I told th’ master,’’answered
the recipient of the letter.
, " I see—one or the other has misunder-
stood. Well, the wording will have to be varied
slightly, because I have addressed—l meant the
writer has addressed—you as 1 my dear son.’ ”
“Thet ’udn’t ’ev done, any’ow, Mr. Pounce,”
grinned the newly-made widower. “Th’ ol’
mother ’ated me as pison—an’ ’sides, < she c’u’dn’b
write. The letter’s got to be from Dick !”
“ Well, it’s fortunate you noticed it now—a
smaller thing than this lias upset a marriage be-
fore now. Bub we’ll go inside, and I’ll make the
correction.” . ry
. ‘But, Mr. Pounce, you’ll ’ev to-’ev th’same
in jL won’t ye, as you ’rote t’other with ? ” •
Ihe Nestor among “ penmen smiled on the
uncultured ignorance of his questioner, and
JONES: “ Hullo, mate! Things seem better with you than they were.”
SMITH: “By Jove, they are, old chappie. Starving in Sydney, I came to
Protectionist Melbourne, got a good billet, live well, and as I buy
my clothes at Crawford King & Co.’s wholesale store, I can turn out
decently for about three notes.”
answered him with the garrulity of the proud
rogue. ;
“ My dear Rudde, you must learn that a piaster
j-m ll i Uslliess '? lievei ’ deterred by such trifling
difficulties as arise from different inks,“and post-
marks, and seals, and signatures. Only appren-
tices and journeymen are compelled to. heed points
of that sort. To the proficient there is but one
insurmountable obstacle.”
May I ax.wot that is ? ” sajd the usurer as he
preceded the Expert into the house. •
“ The watermark, sir, the watermark. My
curse be on the man who invented the watermark!
But for a— — watermark I’d ha’ been one o’ the
wealthiest men in London City, Rudde. ’Twaa
the watermark that undid me, sir.”
And with this aggrieved sigh over the irretriev-
able past, Mr. Pounce set to work to correct the
blunder. And within half-an-hour he placed in
Rudde’s hands that which would pass muster in any
court of law, as a true and exact account of the
death at Hayes, Rent, nine months before, of
Rudde’s wife Mary. “ She prayed for you in her
last moments, Rudde,” said the “ penman “ her
erring husband, y’know, and that sort o’ thing—
it always fetches Parson Marsden.”
“You’re right, Mr. Pounce. Tip the minsters
som’think ’bout repentinse an’ furgtveness, an’ it
does ’em an’ everybody a ’eap o’good.”
* * '*
The preliminary dismissal (to the satis-
faction of Messrs. Slyde, Pounce and Rudde) of
the “just cause and impediment” to the latter’s
legal entrance upon another marriage tie having
been thus effected, only one Thing remained to be
done to consummate the scheme —the choice of
the bride.
According to our modern notions, this particular
thing should have been the first matter attended
to, and not the last. But then, judged in the
light of the System, the ideas of to-day appear
absurdly strait-laced. The principal thing, ac-
cording to the System, was to obtain permission
to marry; the selection of the bride would come
afterwards. The reversal of the process was never
openly tolerated by the authorities, for it would
have implied that very independence of judgment
on the part of the transports which ,it was the
stated design of the System to stamp our.
i “ You’ll come with me, Mr. Pounce, to th’
Factory, an’ give me your advice, sir 1”. asked
Rudde.
“ H’rn, ah ! No, I’m afraid not. Should ha’
been happy, but then if Parson Marsden was to
see me, he’d smell a rat. He knows my—h’m—
profession of old.”
“ W’y ye don’ mean as ’e ever gav’ ye a job ? ”
“No, I can’t truly say that he did. But he
knows I had St. John’s register through my hands,
does the old Parson, and he strongly suspects
my—h’m—proficiency in—-h’m—correcting clerical
errors. So I don’t think I’ll go with you. But
you have my best wishes, and the happy bride, too.
And my blessing also.”
And the scamp went through the pantomime of
blessing a kneeling couple. “Bless you, my
children, bless you ! ”
“ W’y,” admiringly said the fortunate bride-
groom, “ Pa’son Marsden c’u’dn’t ha’ done it
better. An’ now, Mr. Pounce, wot do I owe ye,
for this letter-bisness ? ”
“Oh, my good Rudde, Mr. Slyde and I’ll settle
that between us—l have a running account with
him. Don’t you bother about that.”
(The existence of that “ running account,” let
us say, was the main thing which kept Mr.
Pounce’s corpse out of a quicklimed grave. And
Mr. Pounce well knew it!)
“Thank ye, thank ye,” replied Rudde, “well.
I’ll be off, then, sir, if you won’t come. So good
day to ye.”
And the blesser and the blest parted, the former
to go to Mrs. Walker’s inn to wait for the “ up ”
caravan, and the latter to proceed to the Factory
for the purpose of selecting a bride.
* * *
Mrs. Gordon, matron of the Female
Factory, a short, stout, hare-lipped woman, read
out Rudde’s permit and its official endorse-
ments :
Henry Rudde;
ship, Lord Lynedoch.

servant,' named in the margin,

Memo.— Urgent.
The transport within-named is hereby authorised to
select one (1) wife from the third-class inmates of Female
Factory and House of Correction, Parramatta, and the
matron is hereby instructed to facilitate the proceedings.
The Chaplain of the Factory will officiate. Banns dis-
pensed with, in view of the transport’s early return to his
assigned service ; matron to report selection.
(Sd.) E. A. Slyde, P.M.,
Acting Barrack-Master.
“You are Henry Rudde, per Lord Lynedoch ? ”
queried Mrs. Gordon.
“Yes, ’m,” Overseer Rudde, over whom bash-
fulness was gradually creeping, managed to make
reply.
“You can choose the woman now, but you
can’t be married till the mornin’. Mr. Marsden’s
in town, and won’t return till to-morrow.”
Unkind Fate —thus to deprive Rudde for one
day of his brief honeymoon !

But I’ve only got three days’ leave, datin’
froua to-day, an’, unless I get spliced at wanst,
will ’ev no fun mum. Can’t you let me pick th’
ooman now, an’ we’ll come back to-rnorrer an’
suit th’ Pa’son’s convenunse ?”
At this audacious suggestion the Matron’s
wrath was righteously stirred up.
“ What d’ye mean, man ? I’ve a good mind to
have you locked up till Mr. Marsden comes—
wishing to get a girl away from the Institution
without marrying her. He’d give you twelve
months if I did!”
“ Please, mum, I didn’t mean no ’arm,” expos-
tulated the affrighted Rudde, who had thrown
out the proposal in the innocency of his heart,
and from the depths of his belief that Mrs. G. was
an unusually accommodating personage (as indeed
s h® was to well-to-do men). “ I’d reely bring
th’ girl back to-morrer to be tied up, 1 would,
mum, an’ no lies.”
“How much money have you got?” inquired
the virtuous Matron. “You’ll have to leave some
cash with me. you know, to get the young woman
some things.”
“I didn’t know, mum, I thort as th’ System—
did—did sum’think o’ that kind ! ”
“ Well, I’ve heard of you,” rejoined Mrs. Gor-
don, contemptuously, “as bein’ Mr. Slyde’s over-
seer at his farm. But I always gave Slyde credit
for pickin’ out men with some brains, and not per-
fect fools—like you ! For you must be a fool to
think anything o’ the sort. We give our girls
baby-linen, an’ that’s too much —if I’d my way I’d
give ’em strawy no more—but we don’t give ’em
weddin’ gowns. How much money have you
got? that’s the question.”
By dint of the narrowest economies, the six-
pence per day wages Rudde received from Mr
tilyde, by grace of the System, had mounted up
to twelve pounds, and sundry surreptitious and
unauthorised disposals of cabbages had enlarged
the sum to fifteen. Not much to marry on, thought
Rudde—-but, then, if business at the pound went
well, he’d make as much as that in a month of
married existence.
“Fifteen pounds J—you’ve fifteen pounds, have
you i retorted the Matron. “ Well, now, you’ll
have to give me five o’ that for the girl’s things.”
•■Five !” Mr. Rudde was so appalled that he
could hardly believe his ears. A third of his
wealth for a bride—’twas too much !
“ Yes, five, and the girl as you’re to marry ’ll be
dirt-cheap at the price. She’s a real beauty.
Come, hand over ! ”
“ But I ain’t seen anyone yet, an’ I don’t want
a beauty as much as a real, serviceable y’ung
ooman !’’
“Look here, Rudde, you’ve just got to take
what your master wishes. You don’t know it yet,
o’ course, but I’m not doin’ wrong in tellin’ you
You’ve got to take pretty Rosy Lodge.* Mr,
*>lyde has sent me a note to say that’s what you’re
to do—she’s just the wife for you.”.
With fingers that trembled less from his great
regret at parting with his money than from the
impotent anger at the circumstance that he was
to have no voice in the selection of his spouse,
Rudde counted out some Australasian bank-notes.
“One, two, three, four, five —that’s right,” said
Mrs. Gordon, flipping the last note, “and now it’s
the custom, you know', to give my daughter a
pun’-note, just for luck!” And the additional
pound having been transferred with a gesture that
was more grudging than graceful, Mr. Rudde w'as
forthw'ith ushered into the room variously known
to the patrons of the Female Factory as the
“hitching-room,” the “pairing-room,” and the
“ stable.”
When Governor Darling inspected the Factory,
Father Marsden, w>ho was officiating as cicerone,
gave it a different name. He introduced the
sacred spot to Sir Ralph in this classic way
“ And this, your Excellency, is the ante-
chamber to our Colonial Temple of Hymen !” The
Reverend Samuel’s rhetoric on this occasion was
almost as pretty as when he sentenced a man to
be flogged several successive days till he should
confess complicity in a crime alleged against him.
“In this punishment, my man,” he said, “you
will not fail to observe the chastisement of an
offended Heaven.”
• * •
Huddled i a group at one end of the
long, low chamber, Mr. Rudde perceived a number
of white-capped, print-gowned women—young,
middle-aged, elderly. They were the “ eligibles ”
who had been “ trotted up ” for the benefit, not of
Rudde, whose fate was fore-doomed, but of three
or four “Government men ” who were on similar
errands bent.'
“Rudde gazed at the “ third-classers.” In the
centre was standing just the person to suit him,
if he only dare follow his own inclination—a
woman verging on middle-age, but with some
fairness of feature still clinging to her, and with
a robustness of frame that argued good working
powers. He would have liked to crook his little
finger towards her, but, then, what would Slyde
say?
“ Rosy Lodge,” shrieked the Matron. And
Mr. Rudde and his bride-to-be were formally
introduced.
* * #
“ Rosy, my dear.” sweetly said Mrs.
Gordon, “this good gentleman’s come to marry
you ; he’s Mr. Slyde’s farm overseer.” And then,
turning to the bridegroom elect, she continued,
“This is Rosy, Mr. Rudde. She’ll make you a
good wife, I’m sure. And there’ll be a little one
soon, Mr. Rudde—of course, Rosy’ll come back
hereof or that—and while some men’ud object on
that account, you're too sensible, I’m sure. For
you’ll understand that the child'll be such a help
wton it grows up, and'll save you money ; for the
sergeant (her husband) and Parson Marsden, and
everybody says labour’ll get dearer and not
cheaper. And so, now, my dears,- I’ll just leave
you to yourselves for a bit. And. if you do want
Rosy particularly, Mr. Rudde, and will faithfully
promise to bring her back for parson in the
mornin’ —well, she may go to-night!”
* # #
Stupefied, swindled, Mr. Rudde began to
see daylight. He now comprehended both Mr.
Slyde's desire to see him married, and the full
significance of the instructions Mrs. Gordon had
received that he (Rudde) was to marry Rosy and
no one else. And he rivalled what lie had heard
of a six-months’-old scandal. Rumour in Sydney,
half a year back, had delighted in relating how
Captain Woode, of the —th, had kicked Mr,
Slyde out of his quarters at one o’clock in the
morning, and how he had followed up the ex-
pulsion of the police magistrate by sending his
nurse-maid and housekeeper to the Factory." “on
suspicion of loose practices.” Yes, Rudde thought
he understood.
To make quite sure, he ventured to delicately
interrogate the beautiful little girl who stood
gazing at him with, oh ! so deep a current of
maidenly innocence sunning its surface in her
eyes !
“Was you—l mean, missy—’ad you anythink
to do wi’ Cap'n Woode?”
One of the two was blushing excessively. It
was not the girl, who answered, giggling:—
“ I was his nurse-girl,”
“ But th’ Cap’n—he’s a single man ?”
“ So he is, but I was assigned to him."
“ An’ Mr. Slyde—do ye know ’im ?”
“Rather!” The girl laughed outright at tl
absurdity of the question. She not know Slyde
“ Wot was this I ’erd ’bout the Capt’n an’ m
master?”
“Look here, Rudde, or what’s your name, i :
you want to marry mo, do so, and take me as
am. If you don’t, shut up ! I ain’t going to b
examined by you—you ain’t a beak-or a parson.’
“ Well, you may as well toll mo ’bout the leetlo
un Mrs. Gordon spoke of. Is it Woode’s o
Slyde’s?’’ pursued the projected victim at th
Hymeneal altar.
“I don’t know,” giggled the sweet thing.

“A bargain’s a bargain, Mr. Rudde,” declare!

nasty when crossed.” And—

[Although, in the course of publishing this series of
“ Tales of ‘ The System,’” the fact has been frequently
stated implicitly that each narrative was founded on
actual incidents, the particular conditions reflected in
this story are of such a scandalous nature as to demand
from us a more direct assurance. We may, therefore,
inform our readers (1) that every one of the material
circumstances interwoven with the “ Tale ” is authenti-
cated by official testimony of one sort or another; and
(2) that the conversations which have been introduced to
link historic occurrences together are little more than
paraphrases of either officially-recorded speeches, or, of
the remarks (noted in diaries and other papers to which
“ Price Warung ” has had access) made by the persons
who are the originals of the characters of the story.
When our contributor has deviated from fact, he has done
so in the interests of decency.— Ed. Bulletin.]

Dear Bulletin, —Why is this thus 1 A
“3.C.” teacher of an 8th class N.S.W. State-
school gets “ AC.” pay. A “3.A.” teacher in
charge of a ninth-class school gets ninth-class pay.
This, as the Dutchman says, is a leetle too different
Hal.— Yours, Dominie.
The sentences passed upon Shearer Rae
are unspeakably brutal and utterly illogical from
any point of view save that of savage revenge and
unmixed tyranny. Should Rae bo allowed to
continue in prison his incarceration will lead to
lamentable exhibitions of bad blood between
Labour and Law. If Rae serves any more than
28 days—14 days each for the two admittedly
separate offences committed by him—ho will, if
the N.S.W. authorities wish to be consistent, have
to serve two years and 19 weeks, for if liable for an
hour over the lunar month he is liable for the whole
term. Minister of Justice Gould had better settle
up this serious question without delay, for to
neglect it means a declaration of war to the knife
against every Trade Unionist in N.S. Wales.
The meed of merit for promoting personal
comeliness, is due to J. C. Ayer & Co., whoso
Hair Vigor is a universal beautifier of the hair.
Harmless, effective, and agreeable, it ranks among
thb indispensable toilet articles.
I Dear Bulletin, —What you have said in
Plain English about du Chaillu puts us in mind of
how London Punch ridiculed his little book, with
the picture of dwarfs on the cover. I knew Paul
du 0'., and even I did not quite hold him with the
same respect as a routine flush. Punch’s little
joke was in tho list of new books. One was ;
“Shall you, or shall you not? —an African story,
by Mr. Do Shall You.” And now, what of Stan-
ley? Really I believe him, i.e., I believe him to
be what one of the nigger chiefs christened him—
a couching fire-knob. N.B.—Thirty pounds in
cash and a litter of fox-terrier pups to the first
man who thoroughly sees that joke.— Scotty the
Wrinkler. .
Henry Gawler, son of the early Governor
of South Australia who got so mixed up with the
officials, is a wag in his way. It is related of him
that one night, having missed the train at Gienelg,
he strolled into what he took to be a lecture-hall,
but which turned out to be a Methodist Chapel, and
he found himself in the swing of a prayer-meeting,
much to his disgust. The parson had a full head
of steam on, and when he had blown off he espied
Gawler, who, as usual, was clad in sombre gar-
ments. Thinking him a lay brother he asked our
dear friend to “offer up,” and Gawler, who has a
marvellous memory and a taste for elocution,
tumbling to the humour of the situation, solemnly
delivered the Litany; the dissenting brethren,
who had never heard it, yearningly gloating over
the responses, “Spare us, good Lord,” &c., and
all that sort of thing. After a “ precious time” the
stranger brother was asked to visit them again,
his eloquent prayer having completely fetched the
flock.
There is a coterie of men in Melbourne
who go by the name of the “Lovely Dozen.”
They wear black frock coats, rather shiny, and
bell-toppers, greased so as to look new (at a
distance). But you can never guess their occupa-
tion. The “ Quatorzieme ” of Paris, who holds
himself in readiness to sit at a dinner-party where
perchance the guests number thirteen, is less to
be wondered at than these followers of an excep-
tionally precarious means of obtaining colonial
ale. They are jurymen. Not good men and true
for the trial of etc., but ghouls who sit on bodies.
They go four and four up Lonsdale-street and
along every side of the Hospital that can be got
at, < taking care to keep the entrances in sight.
This is only done when they hear of a death, A
policeman meets four and bail's them up for an
inquest. They protest that they have business
but they go, and just then the other four heave in
sight. In this simple manner they make four
shillings, sixteen pints. They have also the gaol
and the Benevolent Asylum from which the lively
corpse is inquested, and thus we find, in the midst
of death we are in life—S. LE W.
From a Melbourne weekly, which appeals
to the higher culture of the Cabbage Garden and
makes plucky attempts to hoist literature up to
the mental altitude of Chinese settlements, we
cull some sweet-scented blossoms of fresh young
humour. “Galvanised Bob won the oake for
eating duck at the J/erchanics’ the other night;
we think he deserves it after licking Jockey D.”
Again : “Who is the married womens watching the
men at the pub?” Also: “Mary says she has
sphered the barber.” Bouquets composed of
similar flowers of speech are freely slung at pur-
chasers of the above-mentioned periodical, each
bunch being gathered in its own particular dis-
trict. Those under notice came from a place
called Natimuk, but seem only partially charac-
teristic of their birthplace, unless the name given
be a misprint for Nastymuk. In any case, how-
ever, The Bulletin cordially congratulates Mary
on having “spehered” the'barber, because she
must be a lady of original mind to have accom-
plished a feat that is, so far as we know, unparal-
lelled in the history of Victoria.

Another Melbourne coffee palace—the
Grand—is getting shaky in its temperance
principles, and tired of impressing the beauties of
the good cause upon whited sepulchres who have
rum laid on under the bed. At the recent half-
yearly meeting of shareholders in this converted
pub, a sorrowful abstainer announced, with water
in his eyes, that dividends were in the air and the
company had decided to bring down a heavy
deficiency on account, or financial eloquence to
that effect. The Chairman didn’t say there was
no sense in running the Grand as a private
inebriate retreat for such persons as wouldn’t even
sober up on the local fluids or eat on the premises,
but he spoke of retrenchment in a soft, low voice
and wagged his head. And when auctioneer
Bay lee arose to suggest that their only chance lay
in asking the Drink Fiend to return like the
Prodigal to the home of the Reohabite—well, the
meeting heard the Fiend’s supporter without a
shudder. They didn’t repudiate the bare thought
of getting a License to wreck souls and bodies,
but they gazed towards Heaven and seemed un-
commonly doubtful when the next speaker “ did
not think they had reached that stage yd." But
they will reach it. Lack of dividends will drag
them down, down, down to the unhallowed selling
of spirituous liquors, and the price of shares will
fatten on the nourishing extract of immoral
degradation.
London Truth publishes in all seriousness
an American gentleman’s system of telling which
point is the north by looking at one’s watch. The
ingenious Yankee has discovered that if you
point the hour-hand to the sun, the direction of
the South Pole lies exactly half-way between the
hour and figure XII on the watch. As, for
example : If the hour-hand be pointed to the sun
at four o’clock, then the figure II directs exactly
south. Or, at eight o’clock, the figure IV will be
due south. So far, the system does work all
right. At other periods of the day it is apt to be
a snare, however. The writer, at seven o’clock
p.m., pointed figure VII towards the setting
sun and found that south was north. And at six
o’clock, as must be obvious to every searcher after
new truths, there were two half-ways between the
hour hand and figure XII. Besides, neither half
way truthfully indicated the south. After giving
the system a fair trial it doesn’t surprise us to find
that when Stanley was questioned concerning
“ this simple mode of discovering the points of
the compass, he said that he had never heard of
it.” Let that American gentleman call at The
Bulletin office and explain how the dickens his
Waterbury will tell the course of the north wind
at noon, when there is no half way between the
hour-hand and figure XII, and the ticker has to be
held in a perpendicular position. Anyhow, the
alleged system was a fable of our childhood, and
probably existed as an unfounded allegation ere
America discovered itself.
Nehemiah Bartley, of Brisbane, in a paper
read before the local “ Royal ” Society, seriously
maintains that gold grows :— '
About 20 years ago I became possessed of a mineral
specimen from the Wide Bay district. It looked, on the
one side, like a piece of lead that had been melted and
stained by being dropped into fine quartz and gravel; on
the other side it showed a cleaner appearance, like cor-
roded lead, and when cut with a knife ifwas soft, and
•bowed a smooth, bright surface as section of lead-pipe
when similarly cut would do. For 12 years, up till 1882,
this specimen lay alone and unnoticed in a drawer. At
the end of that time a speck of gold showed itself on one
corner of the cleaner surface of the specimen, and in
eight years this speck of gold has travelled nearly half-
way across the face of the specimen, increasing in size as
it went, and is now a patch in place of a speck of gold, and
it has cut a groove for itself as it travelled across the faoa
of the specimen, which groove spreads laterally and in front
as well as behind the travelling patch of gold, not unlike
the track of a caterpillar or a silkworm on the cabbage or
mulberry-leaf respectively. But this is not all. On the
adjoining corner of the specimen another speck of gold
•bowed out for the first time two years ago, but it is too
young yet either to expand or to travel as its eight-year-
old brother has done. I produce the specimen, and offer
no opinion as to the cause of the evolution of the gold
from its matrix, and hope the said specimen will be care-
fully handled so as to avoid abrasion, and leave Nature to,
work out her operation on the face of it, so that all may,
•ee how she does it. I may here mention another curious
fact about a gold specimen. I got it in 1874. It was
from the Etheridge, and was very rich, for it was
picked out of a bucketful of quartz which yielded
450 ounces of gold. The specimen lay for some time in,
a drawer near to a bright lump of chaloo-pyrite
from Wallaroo, South Australia (yellow copper ore). la
course of time the gold on the specimen became coated
with a bright amber and orange-coloured efflorescence,
and it looked like a piece of sulphide of copper with no
signs of gold about it. A dip in nitric acid restored ita
former golden purity of appearance,, and it was kept away
from the bad company of the gentleman from Wallaroo;
but contamination had done its work, and in a few months
out bloomed the “peacock copper ” hues again, only once
more to be slain in the aquafortis ; hut to no purpose, for
the copper “seed ” seemed to have been sown in the gold,
and each period of rest after the acid bath brought out
the sulphurous eruption again all over the nobler metal,
hiding its real nature. I do not know how the matter
would have ended, for I was taken ill after one
of these gold “baths” which revealed the value of the
specimen, and it was “ annexed” by one of the numerous
low class womankind who on various pretexts find
the entree into sick rooms on these occasions. I saw it no
more, and regret that I cannot produce it also for your
inspection ; but, I might ask ( apropos of the above),
“ does copper grow ” as well as gold ? A curious vesicular
rock specimen which I got from New Caledonia, in 1872,
has, of late years, "broken out” into gold in various
places where gold was not visible at all when I first got it.
I produce this for your inspection. It is quite unlike
any Australian rock that I ever saw, auriferous or other-
wise ; but a ton of it gave 64ozs. of gold to a mere barrel-
organ, so to speak, of a crushing-machine. I hope these
specimens will help to clear up some of the mysteries
which surround “ El Oro,” the unobtrusive “littlesister,”
as it were, the Cinderella of the metals, the least gorgeous
in its ore, hut the most indestructible and constant of
them all.
